>
> I am reminded of the Old Saw: 'A lack of planning and foresight on your
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I am reminded of the Old Saw: 'Keep your words soft and sweet, for you may
have to eat them.'

Suppose...just suppose... that you got it wrong. Suppose that SOMEONE you
THOUGHT was looking for Homework assistance (without having actually done
any), was in fact someone really new to newsgroups in general, and this one
in particular, whose only real "sin" was that they requested assistance in a
manner which aroused your suspicions.

Couldn't happen? OK, then I agree that the short shrift meted out to
recalcitrants who come here looking for a free ride is justifiable.
(Although there is still an argument that says no real good purpose is
served by it...)

But, IF there was a case where you got it wrong, then adding a pointer to
the FAQ would at least be of some assistance.
(It would point a genuine knowledge seeker in the right direction and at
least inform him as to what the general rules are...)

And even in the case of the REAL bad guys it wouldn't do them any harm to go
off to the COBOL FAQ and gain some knowledge, would it?

I completely share your distaste for freeloaders, but I honestly believe
that pointing people at the FAQ is a "no lose" situation. If you disagree,
I'd be interested to hear your arguments.

If your argument is simply "I don't want to change the message I use and I
don't need to justify my reasons." That is OK too. (As expressed here many
times, I respect your right (and everybody else's in this forum) to express
yourself however you see fit...)

Pete.

<a id="t-c5e08bb845"></a>
## Thread: Calling PL/I procedures from COBOL? — 1 this month / 3 total

_Thread started [2002-01](2002-01.md#t-c5e08bb845)._

#### ↳ Re: Calling PL/I procedures from COBOL?

- **From:** daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch (Daniel Jacot)
- **Date:** 2002-02-01T00:14:37-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7f3c351f.0202010014.159bd269@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<ec0f6848.0201301941.61d9a43a@posting.google.com>`

```
manojb@mailcity.com (Manoj) wrote in message news:<ec0f6848.0201301941.61d9a43a@posting.google.com>...
> Does anybody know about how to go about calling PL/I procedures from COBOL.
> It's an OS/390 mainframe and I have to write a program which has to call 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks for any replies.


My response to almost every question is 'it depends'. In your case,
the answer depends on the runtime environment (LE or not LE) and the
compiler versions you use.
Supposing a current COBOL compiler, LE and PL/I for MVS, you have to
consider:
- The parameter mapping (unaligned/aligned vs. SYNC, PTR vs. direct)
- The data type mapping (there are PL/I data types without direct
equivalent in COBOL)
- The OPTIONS statement of your PL/I programs

First of all, let's have a look to PL/I source-code. The 'pre-existing
PL/I procedures' might have OPTIONS(MAIN) or OPTIONS(COBOL) in their
PROC-Statement. If they have, the call works fine. Calling a MAIN
program causes LE to create a child enclave which is a performance
issue. The only acceptable OPTION is COBOL. If none of these OPTIONSs
are code in the PL/I program, you cannot call them from COBOL. Period.
If the routines contain a RETURNS statement in the PROC statement, you
cannot CALL them from COBOL since such programs are not procedures but
rather functions. And you will not be able to get the return value set
by PL/I.

PL/I procedures often accept pointers to the parameters rather than
the parameters itself. In a COBOL program, you have to build the
address of the argument and CALL the PL/I routine USING this pointer.
Since both the argument and the pointer reside in the WORKING-STORAGE
SECTION of your COBOL program, you need a subprogram to build this
pointer unless you use the most current COBOL compiler which allows
SET ADDRESS of WORKING-STORAGE items.
Even if you get a PL/I sample how to call the routine, you have to
look at the PL/I source of the routine. PL/I allows the following:
calling program: DCL 01 parameter, 05 ....
                 CALL pliname(parameter);
called routine:  pliname: PROC (ad_parm);
                 DCL ad_parm PTR;
                 DCL 01 parameter BASED(ad_parm), 05 ....
If you CALL this same routine from COBOL, the statement must look like
CALL routine USING ad-parm
where ad-parm is a POINTER to the parameter.
And, as I wrote above, the entry of the routine must be changed to
pliname: PROC (ad_parm) OPTIONS(COBOL);

If PL/I expects a structure (or a pointer to a structure), you need to
have a look to padding bytes. PL/I alignes by default while COBOL does
not SYNCHRONIZE by default. And SYNCH even works differently in some
cases than PL/I ALIGNED does. So, you have to look carefully at the
layout of the structure.

Another problem is the different meaning of the 'S' in the PIC clause.
A COBOL PIC S9(2) mapps to a PL/I PIC '9R'. A PL/I PIC 'S9' mapps to
COBOL PIC S9 SIGN IS LEADING SEPARATE.

You should read carefully the ILC guide. You can access the manual at:
http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com:80/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/CEEA4100/11.0

I would probably write small PL/I interface program, one for each
routine. Every interface has 'OPTIONS(COBOL)' in its PROC statement. I
would then link the interfaces to the COBOL program. The interfaces
then can FETCH the PL/I routine. In this architecture, you can leave
the routines as they are (since they probably are called by PL/I
programs too).

Regards

Daniel
```

---

On 1 Feb 2002 09:02:49 -0800, speedpro@msn.com (Fercho) wrote:

>I have a client runnig an accounting inventory software written on

Hi,

As you didn't mention which vendor you are using, I will assume (based
on the error number) that you are using a RM/COBOL runtime.

As far as I know all versions equal or greater than 5.36 will work.
If the version is older than this one then it is possible it doesn't.

Anyway. Error 98 can be caused for several things, so I sugest you run
whatever recovery program you have supplied with your runtime disks
(you do have them DO you?)j
In the case of RM/COBOL this would be normally "recover1.exe".

If you could give us more information it could help.

FF

Hi Fernando,

It looks like your  client is running RM/COBOL.  Two things that might be of
interest are found in the READ.ME file for
RM/COBOL version 7.00+ for 32-bit Windows.  You did not say what version of
RM/COBOL you are running but these may be something to look at.  They refer
to Windows NT but I think they apply to Windows 2000 as well.    The
extended error code for the 98 error would be useful to know.

          2. Network Redirector File Caching

               This section applies only to Windows NT Server and
               Workstation.

               By default, when the Windows NT redirector opens a file for
               read or read/write access, the redirector uses the Windows
               NT system cache.  As a result, when data is written to the
               file, it is written to the cache and not immediately flushed
               to the redirector.  The cache manager flushes the data at a
               later time.  If an unrecoverable network error occurs while
               the data is being transferred to the remote server, it may
               cause the write request to fail.

               For more information, see the Microsoft Knowledge Base
                Article:

                http://support.microsoft.com
                /support/kb/articles/q163/4/01.asp


          3. Opportunistic Locking on Windows NT

               This section applies only to Windows NT Server.

               With Opportunistic Locking, if a file is opened in a
               non-exclusive mode, the network redirector requests an
               opportunistic lock of the entire file. As long as no other
               process has the file open, the server will grant this
               oplock, giving the redirector exclusive access to the
               specified file. This will allow the redirector to perform
               read-ahead, write-behind, and lock caching, as long as no
               other process tries to open the file.

               Liant Software recommends that this Windows NT feature be
               disabled.

            For more information, see the Microsoft Knowledge Base
                Article:

                http://support.microsoft.com
                /support/kb/articles/q129/2/02.asp

-Rob

## Thread: Where can I learn CICS and DB2 ? — 1 this month / 3 total

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases) · [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

_Thread started [2002-01](2002-01.md#t-82fe04b5b2)._

#### ↳ Re: Where can I learn CICS and DB2 ?

- **From:** 72770.2055@compuserve.com (Gary)
- **Date:** 2002-02-01T18:18:47-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1da191e5.0202011818.3a4f14e3@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<g04e4uoimdbg5q6slunf22a5phofm55eif@4ax.com>`

```
Nomad <abc@Avenue1.com> wrote in message news:<g04e4uoimdbg5q6slunf22a5phofm55eif@4ax.com>...
> Hello,
> I am a Cobol-programmer but so far had only experience with 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> Reinhard
Go to www.murach.com.  They've been selling mainframe texts for many
years - they are very reasonably priced and are designed to get
someone up to speed quickly.  Even experienced folks tend to keep
their stuff for reference, and buy new versions of texts whenever they
do appear.  They have branched out to newer topics lately, but as a
publisher they sell only those things they wrote themselves, and go
for quality rather than quantity of titles.  I have most of them..they
are letter-sized paperback texts, easy to use.  You can't lose.  Where
they have 2-book tutorial texts (yes - CICS and DB2) you will need to
get both.  Can start with their JCL book (their batch scripting
language) but may wish to follow that topic up with the industry's JCL
"bible", "System 390 JCL" by Gary DeWard Brown (do Amazon)
If you do get into an IBM shop and become an IBM "customer", IBM has
online fabulous libraries about EVERYTHING they make (COBOL, CICS,
DB2, etc, etc etc) that are >> FREE << as downloadable PDF books. (The
new stuff too - the UDB version of DB2, WebSphere manuals, MQSeries,
on and on.)  You can have the same libraries their specialists do  . .
on your PC.  Got over a gig on mine at work . . .more than you can
ever read but the trick is to get an idea of WHAT you need to know
(which CAN be scattered thruout that library)and read just that, and
study any topic you need to in whatever depth you need to.  Go to
www.ibm.com and explore a LOT and you may find a lot you can download
- they have improved their developer support to a remarkable level.
(And that's one reason you won't find very much at Borders etc. . . .
there's a virtual warehouse full available and it's all FREE!)
The hard part is learning WHAT you need to know - this was around
before integrated IDE's and each company or dept or more likely team
or developer has their own libraries of reusable code (unfortunately
most companies have never caught on to the reusabiliy thing..usually
developers have to take their own initiatives.)  You will need to know
a number of subjects - the editor, normally ISPF, and JCL, and their
native file structures in addition to DB2 (sequential files and PDS
files and VSAM files), source control programs (differing vendors),
and a number of utilities.  Hit the ones mentioned here to start,
they're the bulk of it.  A lot will be set up for you (like compile
routines)  . .great thing about a mainframe shop is that there
generally are specialists who take a great burden off the developer,
so can concentrate on developing . . sometimes seems a lot easier than
many other environments where one has to try to do it all, like being
an operating system expert, network expert, etc.
 
Do have some free stuff somewhere, and know of some downloadable, will
see if I can round some of it up, may be awhile though.

Hope this is good start - garyh
```

 I am currently involved in working with SOAP and am delighted to find that
Fujitsu Version 6 makes it extremely easy.  (It will generate the necessary
XML "wrappers" without knowledge of this technology being required on the
part of the User)

Thane has been using it for some time and advised me to do so ages ago.

He was right (as he usually is...<G>).

For some time now I have advocated the use of components and have built my
own COM and ActiveX components for deployment on both the desktop and the
Web.  The problem here is that nonMicrosoft platforms have difficulty
deploying these objects. (It can be done with CORBA but it is not an
exercise you would not greet with enthusiasm...).

The Simple Object Access Protocol (SOAP) enables these components to be
"wrapped" in a way that makes them more acceptable than the COM wrapping
that is usually served up with ActiveX. (The term "ActiveX" is falling into
disuse as OLE takes over the functions of what used to be DDE (Dynamic Data
Exchange). The rapid expansion of the whole component based architecture
field has led to much confusion in the various terms. This confusion can be
avoided if you remember that COM means "Component Object Model", in other
words an architecture for building components. They are actually implemented
using On-line Linking and Embedding (OLE) so this is the "wrapper" that goes
arund them and enables them to be served from various servers.)  So, SOAP,
(through the artifice of XML) enables "objects" (components, usually) to be
deployed locally and remotely across different platforms (UNIX, Win32, and
mainframe eventually...)

This is a bonanza for the "mining" companies...<G>  Grab the Legacy code,
wrap it in SOAP, and Hey Presto! Web Enabled Legacy, without any Java in
sight! Makes the COBOL -> Java tools obsolete. (Well, at last as far as
justifying them because it is the ONLY way to Web Enable COBOL...there may
be other, less readily apparent, reasons why you MIGHT want to convert your
Legacy COBOL to Java...)

Of course, if you are using Java you might also want to wrap your Beans in
SOAP to ensure a wider distribution for them. (I think the course that is
advertised in Bill's post (see below) is about using Java with SOAP...pity,
it would be more interesting if it was about using COBOL with SOAP, but I
believe only Fujitsu have integrated this capability into their COBOL
product so far. In fact, it is possible to wrap components written in Java,
VB, C++, DELPHI, or Fujitsu COBOL in SOAP currently.(I don't know about MF,
it should be possible but it may not be easy.)

So why is the mainframe late in getting this technology? Why are they only
talking about using it with Java on S390?

I'll tell you.

Because the whole underlying concept of this approach is OBJECT ORIENTED!

And fortress COBOL programmers (the Dilworths of this world) take to OO the
same way short guys take to nude beaches ("all right for some, but you won't
catch me there..."). (Think about poking your nose into everybody's
business...<G>)

If you intend to be a programmer and you haven't started getting to grips
with OO, you might as well be a Pilot without knowing about stalls and
emergency landings. You'll (probably) get from A to B, but you'll never push
the envelope and you better hope it stays fine...

Learn Java or OO COBOL now! (In fact, learn 'em both...<G>) Yes folks, you
heard it here first (around a year ago now...<G>)

Before we get too carried away with SOAP, realise that this is just an
interim measure to prepare you for bringing your components under the .NET
umbrella and promoting them to WEB SERVICES...

If you develop in .NETCobol there is no need for SOAP. Your components are
already integrated into an environment where the source code (and Language)
is irrelevant. The only importance is the use of the OO paradigm and the
exposure of  Properties, Methods, and Events. Web Services will run on any
platform, remote or local, that has .NET on it. (" 'tis a consummation
devoutly to be wished...")

So, just like the COBOL->Java tools I mentioned in passing above, SOAP too
will be rendered redundant by .NET.

Before anyone parts with hard-earned readies to go on the SOAP course, ask
yourself whether you are going to become an OO Programmer... If you're not,
then my advice would be "Save your money".

But then, my advice would be "Learn OO!". See above...

Pete.

(Peter Dashwood is NOT actively engaged in promoting the learning of OO for
any personal gain or remuneration from any training organizations. He is
giving you this advice for the sake of your career and the good of the
industry. NO Java programmers were injured in the preparation of this
statement. One so-called "COBOL Programmer" may have been...hopefully...I
wish...)
> *********************************************************************
>

"William M. Klein" wrote:

> As I recall, there was a discussion about "what is SOAP" in C.L.C. a while
> ago.  For those who might be interested in this (or other mentioned topics),
> I recently received the following email
>

Your memory is right about SOAP, but wrong group <G> As Pete has indicated it
*was* Thane promoting it.

Now ignoring the various flavours of  getting into the Web, Activex, XML, SOAP,
Components etc., a question for those who are using the Web.

Firstly - BACKGROUND :

Will Price produced a second book, non-OO,  "Elements of COBOL Web Programming",
which was kindly dished out with V3.1 of Net Express. His book is about the
Forms module in the Net Express IDE to design and 'wizard' generate Web COBOL
source and forms/pages.

Fortunately he kicks off with the buzzwords, Client, Server, Protocols, URLs,
HTMLs, CGIs etc. (No mention of SOAP - don't think it was around when he wrote
his book). Although I have no immediate priority to get into the Web, with it
sat on my bookshelf, thought I should give it a read. Absorbed the first part in
great detail, but as I read on, thought the Forms module did not present a big
challenge, once you got into it (Interestingly, unlike the N/E Dialog Editor,
the Forms module allows me to design arrays. Currently I've overcome the Dialog
Editor deficiency by dynamically creating an array using the Entryfield GUI
class).

Second - THE ACTUAL QUESTION

This is where I need clarification. The impression I formed was (a) Your
generate a request form, which you display at the user; (b) the user fills in
the answers and presses <SUBMIT>. At this stage, the COBOL 'control' program
does a complete validation on what is sent back. For example if requested to
fill in say :-

    Name and address info
    phone and e-mail
    Your age group
    Your salary range

If you fail to complete the Name address info or the remainder, then it is
redisplayed at you with error messages.

Other examples could be where you go on-line to a pizza joint and place your
order, either selecting from dropdown lists, checkboxes or radio buttons. Same
sort of thing with Amazon.com where you place book orders in a shopping basket.

The above are fairly straightforward and a common usage of the Web.

Now to a specific problem. Initially DOS but also the same with OO :-

- for quick data entry, user wants to enter numerics without decimal points,
particularly as over 80% are decimal only values. He enters, I store and
redisplay -
Imperial - pic z.999 blank when zero, or Metric - pic zzz.99 blank when zero..

- some of the coding is Mnemonics - he enters "T" and I redisplay 'Top", or "S"
and redisplay "South"

- agreed it depends upon the application but I do have dialogs where the 'core'
represents say, 60% of the display. Other controls are hidden and disabled
unless the user makes a specific selection.

Now Pete may contend that Components handle this very nicely, (built-in self
validation ?). I'd be curious to know.

Sum my query up by -

(1) Is it viable to use the Web for data entry intensive applications,
particularly those geared to numerics as above ?

(2) Net Express users - have you opted for either COBOL Java classes or direct
invokes of Java in preference to the Forms module ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

## Thread: OT (Humor) - ENRON VENTURE CAPITALISM — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT (Humor) - ENRON VENTURE CAPITALISM

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-04T12:52:56-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3mlk1$213$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
 In case you were wondering how Enron came into so much trouble, here is an
explanation, reputedly given by an Agriculture Economics  professor at
Texas A&M to explain it in terms his students could understand.



CAPITALISM:

 You have two cows.

 You sell one and buy a bull.

 Your herd multiplies and you hire cowhands to help out on the ranch.

 You sell cattle.

 The economy grows and eventually you can pass the business on and your

 cowhands can retire on the profits.

>

 ENRON VENTURE CAPITALISM:



 You have two cows.

 You sell three of them to your publicly listed company using letters of
credit opened by your brother-in-law at the bank, then execute a debt/equity
swap with an associated general offer so that you get all four cows back,
with a tax exemption for five cows.

 The milk rights of the six cows are transferred via an intermediary to a
Cayman Island company secretly owned by the majority shareholder who sells
the rights to all seven cows back to your listed company.

 The annual report says the company owns eight cows, with an option on one
more.
```

## Thread: JCL question — 9 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### JCL question

- **From:** "Joe Brault" <jbrault@niu.edu>
- **Date:** 2002-02-04T20:09:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c5eea62_7@news.uncensored-news.com>`

```
Hello,

    I am a Computer Science student at NIU taking a course in COBOL this
semester.  During one of our lectures our professor covered jcl with us, but
didn't say much about it... saying something to the effect: 'everyone uses
it, and so does our server, so you have to include it in your code.'  In my
search for information about jcl, I found a book at my local bookstore from
IBM entitled:  MVS JCL Reference.  I was wondering if anyone could point me
to any references for jcl on the internet?  I would like to get a background
of this language, so I know what it's doing with my code :)  Any help is
appreciated, thanks in advance!

The code hungry student,

 - Joe :)

#### ↳ Re: JCL question

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-04T14:47:42-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3ms1j$i8u$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
There are lots of references for JCL, but what I think you might want to
start with is:


http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3pg00/2.1.1?

which talks about the JCL needed at compile time ("2.1.1 Compiling with
JCL")

You may also want to look thru that manual for various "environments" that
you might want to RUN (execute) your program in (such as CICS, IMS, DB2,
Unix, OO) - and how JCL is used at "execution time".

This "programming guide" relates JCL to "COBOL" programs.
```

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@neo.rr.com>

On 4 Feb 2002 20:09:07 GMT, "Joe Brault" <jbrault@niu.edu> enlightened
us:

>Hello,
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Where or what is NIU?

JCL is a mainframe computer thing..not a server thing.  It stands for
Job Control Language.  There are, in the IBM mainframe world, two
kinds of JCL..one runs under the VSE operating system and the other
runs under the MVS operating system.  You do not put JCL in your code
unless you are generating JCL to be written to a system reader that
will later be run and execute something else.

As for reference, others in this thread have given you some good
examples.

Regards,

          ////
         (o o)
-oOO--(_)--OOo-

I don't have a big ego, I'm way too cool for that.
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

Remove nospam to email me.

Steve
```

:>>The code hungry student,

:>Where or what is NIU?

Northern Illinois University.

One of the few colleges still teaches system programming for IBM mainframes.

:>JCL is a mainframe computer thing..not a server thing.  It stands for
:>Job Control Language.  There are, in the IBM mainframe world, two
:>kinds of JCL..one runs under the VSE operating system and the other
:>runs under the MVS operating system.  You do not put JCL in your code
:>unless you are generating JCL to be written to a system reader that
:>will later be run and execute something else.

Or as a wrapper for his job.

:>examples.

- **From:** jacksleight@hotmail.com (Jack Sleight)

- **References:** `<3c5eea62_7@news.uncensored-news.com> <6ED5D33EB4490D35.644E8757A32C76B0.D75643E16F82B365@lp.airnews.net> <imd06uogjj87ql7jtscn80qjg8plvg1e89@4ax.com>`

```
For what it's worth, I've used the following to delete both tape and
disk PS and PO cataloged datasets.

The ,,DEFER keeps tapes from being mounted and the ops guys from
becoming rabid.  The SYSDA and SPACE is ignored if the dataset is a
tape.  The catalog is used to get the UNIT info. The same goes for the
DCB. They're only used if the dataset doesn't exist.

The SET stmt isn't really necessary, but I keep forgetting the comma
(,) when I overtype the DSN, so I use it.

//DELJOB   JOB etc.		
//         SET MYDSN=my.dsn.for.example
//DELSTEP EXEC PGM=IEBBR14
//DELDD     DD DSN=&MYDSN,
//             DISP=(MOD,DELETE,DELETE),
//             UNIT=(SYSDA,,DEFER),
//             SPACE=(TRK,0),
//             DCB=(SYS1.MACLIB,DSORG=PS)



Binyamin Dissen <postingid@dissensoftware.com> wrote in message news:<imd06uogjj87ql7jtscn80qjg8plvg1e89@4ax.com>...

```

<a id="t-e2c1c5b219"></a>
## Thread: Carriage Return in COBOL Line Sequential File — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### Carriage Return in COBOL Line Sequential File

- **From:** sriprasu@netzero.net (sri)
- **Date:** 2002-02-04T19:33:02-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<24df60cb.0202041933.6c732bab@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi,

I am reading a Line Sequential file.  I want to check if there is any
Carriage Return.  Is there any way to find out if the record read
contains just "Carriage Return".  If a record contains only "Carriage
Return" then, Is the length of the Record will be Zero?

Thanks
```

#### ↳ Re: Carriage Return in COBOL Line Sequential File

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <invalid@invalid.net>
- **Date:** 2002-02-05T03:46:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<FAI78.303219$TC1.21412194@bin5.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<24df60cb.0202041933.6c732bab@posting.google.com>`

```

"sri" <sriprasu@netzero.net> wrote in message
news:24df60cb.0202041933.6c732bab@posting.google.c
om...
> Hi,
>
> I am reading a Line Sequential file.  I want to
check if there is any
> Carriage Return.  Is there any way to find out
if the record read
> contains just "Carriage Return".  If a record
contains only "Carriage
> Return" then, Is the length of the Record will
be Zero?

A. Last question first. A CR is a character. If
the file contains only a CR it will have a length
of 1.

B. One way to discover if a CR exists is to define
the file in another SELECT statement as a
SEQUENTIAL file. In the FD, define this SEQUENTIAL
file's records as something really humoungous.
OPEN the sequential file, issue one READ, CLOSE
the sequential file. Now inspect the SEQUENTIAL
file's record for the CR character (X'0D').

C. Open the LINE SEQUENTIAL file. Blank the record
area. READ a record. If the record area is still
SPACES (and you didn't have a file error), the
record you just read consist of only a CR (or
CR/LF).
```

#### ↳ Re: Carriage Return in COBOL Line Sequential File

- **From:** "Rational One" <noreligion@nogod.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-05T16:41:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5XT78.25731$S06.85722@rwcrnsc53>`
- **References:** `<24df60cb.0202041933.6c732bab@posting.google.com>`

```
you could also check for just a CR by defining the file as binary, not line
sequential
then count characters and check to see if your first character is X"0D" (in
ascii)


"sri" <sriprasu@netzero.net> wrote in message
news:24df60cb.0202041933.6c732bab@posting.google.com...
> Hi,
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks
```

I just try out the cobol language and I found out that they are very

000010 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
000020 PROGRAM-ID.   HelloWorld
000030 AUTHOR.       My Name
000040 DATE-WRITTEN. Jan 23 2002

would be ok.  But AcuCobol ask me to put a period after HelloWorld, My

CALL 'STARTCOM' WITH STDCALL LINKAGE

would cause complain about with STDCALL LINKAGE.

#### ↳ Re: How come AcuCobol and Fujitsu cobol so different

- **Message-ID:** `<LeT78.310022$QB1.22408430@bin3.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`

"VD" <st946tbf@drexel.edu> wrote in message

> Name, and 2002.

## Periods are standard. Fujitsu relaxes the
standard.

>
> Then this call from Fujitsu:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> would cause complain about with STDCALL LINKAGE.

## Don't know. Works on my Fujitsu.


>
> Passing back and forth parameter using by Value
of a COMP-2 variable
> is ok with
> Fujitsu, but causes word boundar problem in
AcuCobol which I don't
> know how to fix.

## COMP-2 is a double-word floating point
variable. AcuCobol may require proper alignment.
Try moving the field to a temporary field at an 01
level.
>
> Please through at me some hints.
>
> I also search this group about information
regarding convert value
> between
> COMP-2 and normal PIC format (like 9(6)V9(2)).
Could some one tell me
> if there is any standard way of doing so?  Is
there a built-in
> function or feature for this?

## Sure. It's called MOVE, as in MOVE MYFLOAT TO
MYDISPLAY. You won't find much in this group (or
the COBOL community) on COMP-2 because COMP-2
usage is very, very rare. In several decades, I've
never seen it used. FORTRAN, however, is a
different story.

>
> I tried, but I got garbage.  However, display
the COMP-2 seems to work
> ok ( I can see the value correctly).

Unless you have some compelling reason to use
COMP-2 fields, don't. You will encounter loss of
precision, stuff won't add correctly, division
kills your computation, etc. Your results will be
accurate with floating point, but not precise. If
you are unsure of the difference between accuracy
and precision, here's an example:

The moon is 240,000 miles from earth (precise to 2
significant digits).
The moon is 243,127.544 miles (precise to 9
significant digits).

Commonly. COBOL program deal with money. People
count money to pennies. They get really, really
upset when you round off their paychecks, even
thought the paycheck is 'accurate.'
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How come AcuCobol and Fujitsu cobol so different

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-05T12:03:43-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3p71o$uqd$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<f4aa172.0202050706.7535d173@posting.google.com> <LeT78.310022$QB1.22408430@bin3.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <f4aa172.0202050930.6a173b48@posting.google.com>`

```
There is a WORLD of difference between ANSI/ISO conforming COBOL source code
(portable between most compilers) and "perfectly valid vendor extensions" to
COBOL.

Things like the optionality of the period after the program name in the
Program-ID paragraph is a "medium common" extension - but certainly not
universal.

For what is "portable" and what is an "extension"

A) Check out your vendor's documentation.  It should indicate what is an
extension

B) run your compiler with an ANSI or FIPS flagging mechanism.  (how to do
this is also different from vendor to vendor - but should also be in their
documentation)

  ***

Currently *all* floating-point support is an extension - and works
differently from compiler to compiler.  (The next ANSI/ISO COBOL
Standard -expected to be approved at the end of this year - DOES provide
support for both "internal" and "display" floating-point data-types.)

Currently *all* methods of specifying "linkage" between COBOL and non-COBOL
(e.g. C) programs is also an extension and QUITE different from vendor to
vendor.   If you check out both of your vendors' documentation, they
probably have "entire sections" on how to CALL and be CALLed by C programs.

One common way to "get around" this difference is to be "vendor extension"
code in COPY members, so you can COPY in the specific "variation" that you
want for each compiler.

P.S. "MYFUNC-RETURN-CODE" should be valid for any/all compilers.  Exactly
WHAT message did you get when you used it - and how did you define that data
item?  My guess is that there was something ELSE wrong - besides the length
of the field name.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How come AcuCobol and Fujitsu cobol so different

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <invalid@invalid.net>

## Thread: acucobol acuserver? — 3 msgs

### acucobol acuserver?

- **From:** "Dennis Edward" <temp081100@ipipeline.net>

#### ↳ Re: S9(3)

- **From:** kedarnj@hotmail.com (Kedar)

- **Message-ID:** `<d5258c4e.0202060438.1911e87f@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3809deb5.0202050858.39cad70b@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi Oliver,
Is the definition only S9(3)?
This IS a string with lenght 3 bytes. The sign however would be stored
in rightmost or units place zone. If you are using it purely for
Display purpose why don't you try SIGN IS LEADING/TRILING SEPARATE
clause? In that case the string will be of 4 characters with sign
appearing before or after the digits depending on the LEADING or
TRAILING cluase used.

hope that helps,

Kedar  
 
oliviersax@ifrance.com (Olivier) wrote in message news:<3809deb5.0202050858.39cad70b@posting.google.com>...

articles" for people who aren't interested is just what I need...<G>

Despite repeated attempts to get people here interested in OO Methodology
and the components that come from this, I have to admit the response has
been....zilch. No-one has ever responded positively and said they are now
looking at OO or Java, and, for the most part, there isn't even comment on
the posts.  Case in point, yours is the only response to a post I spent some
time on, because I realise there is a lot of unnecessary "mystery"
surrounding the subject.

HTML and attach it to the post so people can look at it.

Nada...

On the other hand, there was a very good response (both publicly and
privately) to the article I wrote in April on Aboutlegacycoding.com which
outlined the future of components.

If I believed there was any kind of interest, I WOULD take the time to
provide tutorials and overviews for OO in general, OO COBOL in particular,
Component development, and Web Programming with COBOL.

But I would need a lot more encouragement than I'm getting at the
moment...<G>

The fact is that most people here can't see how important this will be to
their future.

Pete.

Just because I have not posted any replys does not mean I did not find this
thread very interesting.  In fact I saved them to study later.  Also,
because of previous posts I have  recently read both "Elements of OO COBOL"
and "Elements of COBOL Web Programming".  The OO Cobol book was the first
one I ever enjoyed.  All of the others left me feeling ripped off and
wondering if "I just didn't get it".  I was also puzzed because they kept
mentioning new standards that would appear "real soon now", but then never
seemed to show up.  I did "get it" this time and reread the books that
disappointed me.  I would say that they were more of a disappointment than
ever.  I have also bought Murach's book on Java and plan on reading it
soon.  I would have read it by now but I am having an installation problem
due to a lack of space on my C: drive.

I try to keep current and this newsgroup and the Internet in general have
helped me tremendously.  In the past I was always suprised when a new
release arrived, but now I can find out about them well in advance. It
helps with motivation when you actually have the software to try out the
new advances.  My motivation would also be stronger if I had any idea in
hell what  direction my organization was going in.  At one time it was C++
and client-server so I took a C++ and Visual C++ course.  That was the kiss
of death as management decided that approach was too costly.  It did help
me undersatnd OO concepts and terminology.  Anyway I enjoy learning new
things.

I would say IBM's SOM was a dismal failure in promoting OO Cobol.  I am not
aware of a single Cobol book on SOM.  In fact I asked on this newsgroup if
anyone new of such a beast and the only referral I got was to the manual. 
I did find a web site that had information but some of that was
discouraging. In the past the manuals have not been very available to me
but now the Internet provides them to me.  I was reading the programmer's
guide and strongly motivated to reach the OO part when IBM announced that
SOM was basically dead.  Thanks IBM for saving me from wasting my time.

My last excuse for not posting is that I am not a fast typer and a post of
this size takes me anywhere from 20 to 30 minutes.  I have to do a lot of
rereading, rephrasing, and typo corrections.  I tend not to post unless I
feel I have something to contribute, but I am always lurking, to learn
somthing new.

<snip>
```

Taking your first paragraph - you are dead right !. I can't remember whether or
not I got the other OO books 'before' or 'after' reading Will's books, (plus Ed
Arranga/Frank Coyle - theirs preceded Will's offerings).  The 'other' books are
'not disappointing' but a real come on. One even mentions, without detail,
collections/dictionaries - based on communicating with some J4 people - and he
spells out the hierarchy - and that is taken from what was a 'tentative'
document prepared by Russell Clarke (Australia), back in '96. Fairly recently
Bill Klein gave J4 a nudge about this document. Russell  as far as I can
gather, has given up on COBOL and now works primarily in Java. He put one hell
of a lot of effort into his initial paper.

Additionally, and like Will's, Ed's book is also EXCELLENT if you haven't read
it. But again with some inside knowledge he also referred to a COBOL database.
I haven't seen hide nor hair of that one and the 'real soon' you mentioned was
meant to be 1996 /97 - enough said on that topic.! As it stands, J4 is pretty
firm on OO for December 2002 with the exception of collections/dictionaries,
which is still up for grabs. Hopefully, without being partisan, J4 may arrive
at a collection hierarchy which is an amalgam of Fujitsu, Hitachi and Micro
Focus existing classes. Let's face it, it makes absolute sense to work from
something that is *known/proven* to work and fine tune it, rather than start
over fresh with a blank piece of paper. I'm sorry, but IBM doesn't get a look
in.

Incidentally, there's a pretty good chapter, (not exhaustive) on OO in 'COBOL
Unleashed" if you have a copy. I think Thane advised me that was written by
somebody in Scandinavia - that keeps it 'broad' 'cos I can't remember which
country <G>.

>
> I try to keep current and this newsgroup and the Internet in general have
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> <snip>

Ah well - I was trained as a stenographer in the RAF - no idea what speed is,
but a fair bet I'm quicker than you. I used to write B-E-A-U-T-I-F-U-L.
shorthand - only snag, like C++, I couldn't translate back what I had written
<G>

Jimmy

University Edition to full blown N/E V 3.1. With their already existing
knowledge of OO, (and *ASSUMING* they actually got jobs in COBOL <G>), your
ideas on Components would give them a whole set of new thoughts. Same applies of
course to youngsters using the F/J Tutorial Edition, (I think there is now a
version, isn't there, or at least one in the works ?).

You CARE about COBOL - and the youngsters represent its only salvation from
becoming defunct - putting aside you 'futuristic' comments that specific
languages will be dead.

A thought - write Will Price, putting out a feeler.  I think he's got the pulse
on what is happening in academic circles in the US.

Be a week or so before we get any response from Thane - he's just about to
whistle off to a J4 meeting. Still, Charles did put his two cents worth in.

Meanwhile - Speak Up ! Calin in Toronto - I *know* you are using N/E with the
Web. You had the arrogance to admit to it <G>. Give us your
impressions/approach.

Jimmy

On  6-Feb-2002, Susan A Allen <susan.a.allen@boeing.com> wrote:

> I am fascinated by the topic -- but since I support legacy code and will
> for some time; I can only dream of some day doing OO in COBOL.

I got a masters on OO two years ago, but am in a similar situation as you. 
I am known on this forum for my arguments against enterprise level OO, but
my wish is to lose those arguments - badly enough to not only be convinced
myself - but with sufficient arguments to persuade my workplace to work with
mainframe OO CoBOL.  While I personally am not far off (probably because I
would ENJOY the project) - I am light years away from having arguments that
can persuade an enterprise.

I'm afraid OO CoBOL for the enterprise will back in with web services as a
step sister of Java, and the only reason it will be used is because
companies already have CoBOL programmers.  Nobody will convert the important
stuff over.

The best hope of satisfying us in this regards would be if we bought a
business system that was designed with OO CoBOL as a major component.  But I
don't think anybody's contemplating that.

Howard Brazee <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote in message
news:a3s3c8$3fd$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On  6-Feb-2002, Susan A Allen <susan.a.allen@boeing.com> wrote:

> would ENJOY the project) - I am light years away from having arguments
that
> can persuade an enterprise.
>
As a corporate consultant myself, Howard I have great sympathy for this
argument.

It is difficult to persuade corporates to take risky steps. Your credibility
is on the line and if they fail (for whatever reason) possibly your job too.
(While I don't have a career to worry about, I do have a track record to
consider. At least if I want to keep working (and I do for a few years yet).
I suffer from the same anxiety as anybody else in this situation, namely, if
you screw up they only remember the one you got wrong and immediately forget
all the stuff you did right...<G>. I guess this keeps us honest, but it can
also tie us into not taking risks when we arguably should.)

However, there ARE demonstrable positive benefits from bringing in OO. Even
OO COBOL. While it is wordier and more unwieldy than Java, it DOES retain
all the accepted benefits of COBOL, plus it provides the accepted benefits
of OO in general (which Java programmers take for granted and which we have
debated at length in this forum...). There ARE risks (the learning curve can
be horrific) but, provided the risks are evaluated and managed, the rewards
can be well worth it.

I think the answer is small, low risk, pilot projects to get programmers
around the learning curve. Once a few of these are under your belt and
people see how maintenance can be eased by Class extension (for example) it
is possible to generate some enthusiasm for the new paradigm.

If it doesn't start somewhere it will never happen.

(In this event many people will say, "So what...we don't need it. We get
along OK with what we're doing..." This fails to take into account the fact
that the technical and Business environments are in the process of radical
change. If you close your eyes to these changes there won't BE an Enterprise
to continue doing what it has always done, ITwise...)

The clincher is that if your vendor doesn't encourage OO COBOL, and it looks
like IBM are definitely in this category, then you are shovelling shit
uphill before you start.

If there is no compiler available then there is no argument...


> I'm afraid OO CoBOL for the enterprise will back in with web services as a
> step sister of Java, and the only reason it will be used is because
> companies already have CoBOL programmers.  Nobody will convert the
important
> stuff over.
>
> The best hope of satisfying us in this regards would be if we bought a
> business system that was designed with OO CoBOL as a major component.  But
I
> don't think anybody's contemplating that.

Not today, maybe. I wouldn't mind betting that within 10 years you will be
using components and/or Web Services, on your mainframe, that were written
with languages that won't even compile on your mainframe today, maybe even
OO COBOL...

Pete.

Hi,

I have been looking around for decision table processors for C or Java but
on the web I have only been able to find Cope for  cobol.  Does any one know
if there is an equivilant for C or Java.

```

#### ↳ Re: Decision Table Processors

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-02-06T04:34:11+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C60AFFD.5342B300@shaw.ca>`
- **References:** `<1z_78.2540$sg.58234@news.deakin.edu.au>`

```


Keith Lockwood wrote:

> Hi,
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks Keith

Keith,

Thanks for pointing us at Cope for COBOL which is from Australia. Tried a Web
Ferret search "Java Decision Tables", "Decision Tables Java" - zilch.

But I did get a couple of hits on "Decision Tables C". May not be what you are
after, but who knows ?

Are you familiar at all with the work of Brian Henderson-Sellers - university
based Down Under ? His topic appears to be Components.

As regards Cope, I noted it claimed that you could dramatically reduce your
COBOL source. Have you used it effectively with COBOL ? My reaction was,
although in theory it is supposed to be possible, to view a business problem as
a totally mathematical model - it raises some doubts in my mind as to the
complexity it may cause. Inevitably, if you use an extra tool, it is going to
'push' your mind down an established path, just so your design fits the tool.
(In that respect, COBOL is so open ended, allowing you to travel many paths to
achieve the same solution).

I'll accept that discrete modules in a program can be preprocessed as they
claim, but a whole program or application - Oooohhhh ! (And as somebody here is
want to quote from time to time, "It's the artist, not the paintbrush". And I
don't consider that flip, it truly applies to a skilled COBOLer).

As an example - somebody quite proficient in OO COBOL, 'wrapped' his COBOL file
handling classes within the tool Rational Rose. For the life of me, can't
understand why he did that - it is dead easy just using OO and standard COBOL
syntax.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-05T23:59:20-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3qgvk$i2b$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<a3puc1$f9h$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3c609e0f_11@Usenet.com>`

```
What this means is that they are dropping "support" (service) for IBM COBOL
for OS/390 & VM V2R2 - and REPLACING it with "IBM Enterprise COBOL for
OS/390 & z/OS" (for the "MVS" world - the VM and VSE sides of the mainframe
have *other* issues/releases).

What is particularly interesting is that IBM *seems* to be "pushing forward"
on trying to get all their COBOL customers on "current" compilers.  Some of
the differences between the "newest" compiler (available since last year)
and the one for which they will drop support at the end of 2004 (hardly
"instantaneous") are:

1) "old" compiler used SOM for their OO support; new is Java based and
*only* works in their Unix "mainframe" environment (Unix System Services)

2) "old" compiler still supported much of the '74 Standard (where it
differed from the '85) via the CMPR2 option; new compiler no longer supports
this.

3) *BOTH* compilers require "LE" (Language Environment) for the run-time
(so similar to .NET in "design" that you wouldn't believe that M$ wasn't
following IBM).  However, the new compiler requires a "pretty current"
version of LE (V2R10 - or higher).

  ***

NOTE WELL:
  There are *almost* NO "syntax" enhancements in Enterprise COBOL to bring
in "new features" from the draft COBOL Standard. HOWEVER, its OO support is
a "little" closer - but as it REQUIRES Java and USS, I don't see it as
"particularly" strategic for IBM mainframe OO use.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-02-07T02:35:58+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c613389_11@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<a3puc1$f9h$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3c609e0f_11@Usenet.com> <a3qgvk$i2b$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Thanks Bill.

Informative , as always...<G>

It is very interesting to see IBM committing to Java as their OO and Web
Based tool.

There is, of course, nothing wrong with this, but it would have been nice to
see some more support for OO COBOL. I guess they are going with the flow...
If their COBOL customers for the most part can't/won't embrace OO, and they
can't get Web Enablement without OO, their only remaining  (viable) option
is to use Java.(By now, most sites have realised that commercial development
in C++ (for anything other than trivial or very specialised applications) is
a recipe for disaster, and C# is yet to set the World alight...)

I can see a split in the camp with the "data processors" sticking with Batch
COBOL and the Web Enablers going to Java instead. The bright newcomers will
want to be where the action is, so we will see more people learning Java.(No
bad thing.)

The combination of .NET in the Client/Server arena, and the use of the
common LE environment on the mainframe will obviate the emphasis on source
code anyway, and it won't really matter what language they use. The last
bastion of fortress COBOL will be the overnight Batch processing, and as
real time transaction rates steadily increase, it is arguable how much of
that will be required in a few years time...

It is really a tragedy how COBOL has missed the boat in this environment.

We have an elegant, powerful, language, superbly equipped for the task of
business application development on the mainframe, the desktop, and the Web
and it will be sidelined. Partly because of its public perception (and this
is only partially deserved), partly because Users are demanding open systems
that can interface with standard software, and partly because there are not
enough sites prepared to embrace the innovations and use it to the extent of
which it is capable. ("I didn't know you could do THAT with COBOL!...")

I intend to continue churning out components in it as long as it remains the
best tool for the job (and it is for MANY jobs).

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ LE vs .NET (was IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-07T21:01:35-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3vf2v$ueo$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<a3puc1$f9h$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3c609e0f_11@Usenet.com> <a3qgvk$i2b$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <3c613389_11@Usenet.com> <bfdfc3e8.0202071849.7b6cadbb@posting.google.com>`

```
Why do I think that LE is like .NET and vice versa?

Let's look at their "design goals" (as I understand them)

1)  Provide a SINGLE "run-time environment" for multiple programming
languages

2) Provide a set of "documented" interfaces so that HLL (High Level
Languages) can access "operating system services"

3) Include support for single-interface - multi-language development and
debugging environment

4) Include "open" and "documented" environment so other vendors can provide
"conforming" implementations

5) Intended for a "single" proprietary operating system - where the
environment (LE or .NET) is "closely" tied into the operating system - so
that (eventually) everyone who has the operating system, also can run
applications "developed" to the "interface".

  ***

OK, Thane (via CLC) - what am I missing?

YES, ".net" doesn't use APIs to get to the services; they use
classes/object.
  Yes, IBM's product came out a decade before the M$ product
    yet, originally IBM claimed it would port LE to OS/400 and OS/2 - but
those are still proprietary

So, again, WHERE/HOW do these two "things" differ in what they are TRYING to
do - and even to a great extent how they are doing it.
```

I have little experience with LE but a couple of .NET aspects not mentioned
in the comparison:

1. Access to the 'API' is via an object model ONLY

2. Object sharing for client-server and/or WWW applications is builtin (via
SOAP and XML)

3. All executables are self-documenting components/extensions to .NET

4. The CLR is type safe

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: LE vs .NET (was IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-02-08T11:13:39-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a4110e$hrt$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<a3puc1$f9h$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3c609e0f_11@Usenet.com> <a3qgvk$i2b$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <3c613389_11@Usenet.com> <bfdfc3e8.0202071849.7b6cadbb@posting.google.com> <a3vf2v$ueo$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <a3vh0u$dm4$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
I do want to expand (a bit) on my original note.

A) The actual *implementation* of .NET and LE is *totally* different (both
from the "internals" and the programming interface).  I fully
accept/understand this.  My point is/was that the "intent/design" is VERY
similar.

B) As I did indicate in my original note, I understand that LE uses "APIs"
for accessing OS services while .NET uses OO facilities.  In my PERSONAL
opinion, that is not necessarily a "good" thing as it makes it difficult to
take existing programs and "move them" into the new environment.   It
certainly means that existing ANSI/ISO ('85) Standard COBOL programs canNOT
take advantage of this environment (nor can "new" .NET programs be
considered portable to OTHER existing ANSI/ISO conforming compilers - as
opposed to LE where this IS possible).  It also (as I understand it) means
that even C programs cannot be "enabled" for .NET; I am not even certain
whether C++ (as opposed to C#) programs can use the new environment.  I do
*not* consider it "desirable" to FORCE application programs into proprietary
*and* non-portable source code to take advantage of "enhanced" environments.
In actual fact, this WAS one of the problems with the original LE, that to
TAKE ADVANTAGE of the new features, you needed to "upgrade" your compiler.
(Although at least with LE the "new" source code was still portable to
non-IBM compilers).

C) I do NOT think that forcing programs into an OO paradigm is a good
thing - any more than I think that FORCING programs into a GUI interface is
a "good thing".  Often it is an EXCELLENT choice for new applications (or
even upgraded old ones).  However, that is something that the individual
"shop" (programmer? analyst?) should decide *not* the OS vendor.

D) "strong typing" is a "nice thing" in MANY cases, but definitely NOT in
all cases.  It is one of the things that is GOOD about COBOL (IMHO) is that
it allows the PROGRAMMER to decide this type of thing.  I *do* think that
the draft COBOL Standard is a GREAT improvement in that it SIGNIFICANTLY
enhances the ability for portable COBOL source code to use "strong typing".
Furthermore, it provide EXCELLENT run-time (as well as compile/bind time)
support for "detecting" type violations.   However, no (future conforming)
COBOL compiler will be able to FORCE strong typing.  If .net does this, then
(again) it will be forcing non-ANSI/ISO conforming source code.

E) I fully understand/accept that what was "good" and "expected" for a
"common run-time" on an IBM mainframe in the early '90s is *not* the same
thing as what is "desirable" in a Microsoft environment in 2002.  I don't
expect .net to provide "built-in" support for CICS, IMS, or even VSAM -
anymore than I expect LE to "expect" that all users use GUI interfaces and
have OO classes available to them.

   ***

Bottom-Line:
  .NET and LE applications/programs will look TOTALLY different.  Certainly
the capabilities of the new "environments" are (and probably will always be)
quite different.  Even the "paradigms" and "development environments" look
different.
  HOWEVER, I still maintain that what the two are "trying to accomplish" FOR
THEIR OWN operating systems is BASICALLY the same thing.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: LE vs .NET (was IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

William M. Klein <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message

Agreed.

> opinion, that is not necessarily a "good" thing as it makes it difficult
to
> take existing programs and "move them" into the new environment.
OK. But in order to get future benefits, it is sometimes necessary to
radically change the status quo. A line had to be drawn as to whether future
development would be OO or not.  If we are concerned about the present (and
we always are at the present) then we can bewail the "difficulty" to move
existing programs into the new environment. In a few years time we will
forget all about this difficulty as we are reaping the benefits of moving,
and all our then current development uses the no longer new environment as a
matter of course.

>  It
> certainly means that existing ANSI/ISO ('85) Standard COBOL programs
canNOT

Well, I dispute your emphasised NOT there, Bill. Standard COBOL programs can
and are being "mined" for use on the Net. You are correct that they cannot
be used in their present form, but many Enterprises are biting the bullet
and moving to the extraction of Legacy code. Sadly, Java rather than COBOL
is being used for the "Wrapper" to make this possible when OO COBOL could do
so very elegantly. Hence the requests we see here for "COBOL to Java"
information. The actual code is not being converted (it is in SOME cases,
using tools like PerCOBOL...) but Java is certainly being used to enable it
for the Web.


> It also (as I understand it) means
> that even C programs cannot be "enabled" for .NET; I am not even certain
> whether C++ (as opposed to C#) programs can use the new environment.

C programs CAN be enabled for .NET but they require conversion to OO
(C++/C#) just as COBOL programs require conversion to OO (or being wrapped
in an OO wrapper).

>  I do
> *not* consider it "desirable" to FORCE application programs into
proprietary
> *and* non-portable source code to take advantage of "enhanced"
environments.
> In actual fact, this WAS one of the problems with the original LE, that to
> TAKE ADVANTAGE of the new features, you needed to "upgrade" your compiler.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> thing - any more than I think that FORCING programs into a GUI interface
is
> a "good thing".  Often it is an EXCELLENT choice for new applications (or
> even upgraded old ones).  However, that is something that the individual
> "shop" (programmer? analyst?) should decide *not* the OS vendor.
>
Well, I agree with you about not forcing people to do ANYTHING <G>. However,
in a commercial environment it is not the OS vendors who do the "forcing";
it is the Market place. The OO paradigm is perceived as being the "best
shot" we have to meet the Market pressure. We simply HAVE to stop
re-inventing the wheel and get truly reusable code, that can be easily
extended to meet new requirements without havng to write it over, and that
can be easily maintained in one place. Hence the steady ascension of
Component technology. And that is dependent on OO Methodology.

As for forcing people into a GUI environment, that is what the Market wants.
It really doesn't matter what we think as technicians. If you want to sell a
product you better find out what people want and deliver it. The success of
the GUI system is testified to daily by over 200 million people. Try telling
them we are going back to command line interface and see how far you get.
You might as well tell a ferrari driver that this model T Ford is just as
effective because it gets you from A to B...


> D) "strong typing" is a "nice thing" in MANY cases, but definitely NOT in

then
> (again) it will be forcing non-ANSI/ISO conforming source code.
>
Oh Gee, so we better not use .NET. Wouldn't want to violate an ANSI
standard... Even if it has done nothing but damage to the case for COBOL,
has no real credibility, and takes 18 years to produce a draft. Get over it.
No one gives a rat's arse whether their COBOL conforms to the ANSI standard
as long as it does what they want it to do. The World is moving on and the
importance of Source Code is diminishing every day. People stuck in the old
days of Source Code being everything just haven't seen it yet. (God knows,
I've tried hard enough to wake 'em up...<G>). Archaic, cumbersome,
non-responsive organisations like ANSI are going to be rendered irrelevant
and rolled over by the Market pressures.

> E) I fully understand/accept that what was "good" and "expected" for a
> "common run-time" on an IBM mainframe in the early '90s is *not* the same
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

No comment. <G>

They do today. Give it 5 - 10 years...

FOR

perhaps...

COBOL 85 is fully implemented in .NET.  There are no changes due to
the .NET infrastructure necessary to compile and run a COBOL 85
program under .NET.

My personal opinion is that anything you read here about .NET from
certain individuals may not be entirely accurate.  Pete, use a healtly
portion of salt when reading such postings.

- **Date:** 2002-02-08T12:34:21+00:00

```
One major difference between .NET and LE is that under .NET, program
_source_ code is transmitted between servers (hosts) and clients (users) and
compiled 'on the fly'  by the CLR on the target machine.

(FWIW,  Windows(r) itself is much closer to LE than is .NET: - common,
documented high-level API access to system services without regard to
program source code language.)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IBM to withdraw SERVICE for COBOL for this-and-that V2 & VisualAge PL/I V2

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-02-08T19:52:05+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c6378ac_6@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<a3puc1$f9h$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3c609e0f_11@Usenet.com> <a3qgvk$i2b$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <3c613389_11@Usenet.com> <bfdfc3e8.0202071849.7b6cadbb@posting.google.com>`

```

Thane Hubbell <thaneh@softwaresimple.com> wrote in message
news:bfdfc3e8.0202071849.7b6cadbb@posting.google.com...
> Not picking on your post Pete -- but it's the latest.
>
What does this refer to Thane? What is the latest?
(And feel free to pick away...it's a public unmoderated forum; I know the
risks in posting here..<G>)


> It's not like IBM doesn't offer COBOL anymore - and I'm certainly not
> opposed to "steering" customers to the most recent releases - for many
> reasons.
>
Again, I don't know what context this is supposed to be in...

> The comment about .NET being like LE is just quite silly however.
> It's about like saying a Cessna is like a 747.  Well, they do both
> fly.  LE and .NET are worlds apart.
>
The original comparison was drawn by Bill Klein ( he suggested that
MicroSoft might have been watching IBM because of the conceptual similarity
betwen LE and .NET.) I don't think that's silly. I can see a number of
"common features" they each provide within their respective environments.

What I was getting at is that both of these systems provide a common
generated code base for various languages. You can develop on a mainframe in
PL/1, COBOL, or whatever, and the generated code (linked with LE) will be
almost indistinguishable. The MSIL inherent in .NET allows development in a
plethora of Source languages too but, again, it would be very hard to tie
the intermediate object code back to any particular language source (unless
you specify at the time you write it that that is what you want it to do,
maybe for debugging alongside source code...)

As you know, Fujitsu already provide multi language debugging, moving
effortlessly from COBOL source code to VB or C as various modules are called
or Methods invoked.

Because of this move towards "universal object code" the linking and
combining of modules developed in different source code is facilitated.
Hence my argument that the days of Source Code being King and it being
necessary to develop in ONE language for ease of maintenance, are fast
coming to an end.

You know as well as I do (probably even better) that once something becomes
a Web Service it is irrelevant what language it is written in. Provided it
reveals its Methods, Properties, and Events, it is usable by one and all.

How long do you think it will be before the mainframe joins this scenario? I
can see .NET being run on mainframe servers in the not-too-distant future.
The LE environment can easily be extended to accommodate MSIL if IBM decided
to go that way, and even if they don't it is very likely that SOMEBODY will
provide MSIL support on the mainframe. All of these "Common Environments"
are just steps towards a "Universal Platform Independent" environment where
the system will decide what platform it is to run on (at run time) and JIT
link for that platform, mainframe or Client/Server. (This will be true
peer-to-peer networking when it arrives...)

While I agree the similarity between a Cessna and a 747 may be their ability
to fly, I'm not sure that is the end of it. They both use the same
navigation aids... In other words, if they are both heading in the same
direction they are probably tuned to the same VOR...<G>

The "beacons" driving the IT industry are the same, irrespective of the
platform you happen to be "flying" on.

Users want smarter OSes and friendlier applications. The power of Networking
in general and the Internet in particular is probably the best way to attain
those goals.

I stand by what I said below. COBOL will be sidelined  to commercial Batch
Processing  on mainframes (OK, I guess CICS will be around for a while
too...<G>) unless the language is demonstrated to be capable of working in
the new environments and there is some major shift of attitude by many COBOL
shops and people.

It is really all down to OO. There is no network without it. And the network
is the future. COBOL without viable OO is a non-starter. The OO languages
will thrive, the non-OO languages will die.

End of story.

Pete.



>
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:<3c613389_11@Usenet.com>...
> > Thanks Bill.
> >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> > There is, of course, nothing wrong with this, but it would have been
nice to
> > see some more support for OO COBOL. I guess they are going with the
flow...
> > If their COBOL customers for the most part can't/won't embrace OO, and
they
> > can't get Web Enablement without OO, their only remaining  (viable)
option
> > is to use Java.(By now, most sites have realised that commercial
development
> > in C++ (for anything other than trivial or very specialised
applications) is
> > a recipe for disaster, and C# is yet to set the World alight...)
> >
> > I can see a split in the camp with the "data processors" sticking with
Batch
> > COBOL and the Web Enablers going to Java instead. The bright newcomers
will
> > want to be where the action is, so we will see more people learning
Java.(No
> > bad thing.)
> >
> > The combination of .NET in the Client/Server arena, and the use of the

source
> > code anyway, and it won't really matter what language they use. The last
> > bastion of fortress COBOL will be the overnight Batch processing, and as
> > real time transaction rates steadily increase, it is arguable how much
of
> > that will be required in a few years time...
> >
> > It is really a tragedy how COBOL has missed the boat in this

> > is only partially deserved), partly because Users are demanding open

> > enough sites prepared to embrace the innovations and use it to the

> > which it is capable. ("I didn't know you could do THAT with COBOL!...")
> >
> > I intend to continue churning out components in it as long as it remains
the
> > best tool for the job (and it is for MANY jobs).
> >
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…

> > > "instantaneous") are:

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

run-time

wasn't

Lee Unterreiner <flu@nospam.flu-ent.com> wrote in message
news:a402hn$ctc$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net...
>
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > is the future. COBOL without viable OO is a non-starter. The OO
languages
> > will thrive, the non-OO languages will die.
> >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> The only technology that has ever shaken my faith in COBOL was Visual
Basic.
> Fujitsu fought back 3 years ago with PowerCOBOL.  .NET takes the concept
to
> another level.  Virtually any class in any language is by its very nature
a
> component, usable by other programmers, and seamlessly integrated into the
> designers, editor, debugger, compilers, etc.
>
Lee, if you knew the hours I have spent preparing papers, writing articles,
answering questions, researching and experimenting on this very subject you
would know that you are not just preaching to the converted; you are
preaching to the Choir Master <G>!

Components are definitely the future. I said this 3 years ago when I first
struggled to create ActiveX components with PowerCOBOL Version 4.  The best
exposition of the arguments that I have done to date, was in April last
year. Please read the article I wrote called "Meet the Future". (Go to
www.aboutlegacycoding.com and press the "ARCHIVES" button. Then select the
April 2001 edition. It is the lead article.)

Now, as we see Components evolving into Web Services and these, in turn
becoming available to anyone who needs their Methods and Properties, you are
right that these Components transcend Languages and Environments.

This, in effect is causing a refocus on the Business Functionality, rather
than the technicality of producing Business functions in code. When the
"building blocks" are pluggable, and easily incorporated into the user
interface on either the desktop or the Web, then it is possible for Business
people to use them, rather than just programmers. (I have found that teams
comprised of both are very effective, but I expect this to change in favour
of the Business in the future.)

I see a time when Business functionality will be implemented by various
departments of the Enterprise who really know the Business and know just
what functionality they need (or will interact and experiment with a Visual
Environment until they see what they want...trial and error, build with
blocks, knock it down and do it again until it is right...).

At this point computer power will really be in the hands of the People (as
opposed to the Technocrats), and all of us can consider the job
finished...<G>

Components, and the easy interfacing and implementation of them will make it
possible.

Pete.

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

>
> Now, as we see Components evolving into Web Services and these, in turn
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> possible.

Well I must protest. Although I'm with you generally I think you let your
enthusiasm for the topic carry you over the top. What you are saying was written
in a different sense by Gerry Garfunkel in his 'COBOL 85 Examples'. To
paraphrase, "Users would be 'empowered' by pre written software, such as
spreadsheets DBs etc.".

You are both right, and there are a lot of users already doing this, and I'll
put money on it, doing much of it in a grossly inefficient way, with a lot of
that knocking down and rebuilding you refer to. What's important here is not
"us" (the technocrats)  and  "them" the users, but rather, and this was the
focus point where I started in EDP - the 'well being" of the corporation for
which "us" or "them" are providing the service.

No doubt you'll respond that IT departments can be just as inefficient - no
doubt there are some - but that is the problem of the managers ABOVE the IT
department. Yes I like your idea of mixed teams achieving this through
components. Like all businesses you need tecchies in the background; there is
always that minor technical specification that can throw a loop into the design.
How often here, do we see among ourselves, "How do I use "X" with "Y" to do the
following in COBOL ?".

IT Departments should be run on a budget to judge their cost effectiveness to
the organization. Take a large chunk of their activity away giving the users
components to substitute - exactly how do you measure that the Purchase
Department, Accounts Payable, Receivables, Treasury, Inventory,
Distribution/Transportation and the big boo-boo - Marketing - are working
effectively and efficiently, (i.e. for the lowest dollar cost output). ? You
can't just give the end users a tool - it's effectiveness has to be measured.

You can take for granted, at this point in time, such technical tools will not
be included in the weightings for judging the effectiveness of Cost (not Profit)
Centres. Some five years down the road, some aspiring management consultant is
going to realize this has never been questioned before - now he writes a book,
makes a name for himself, and bucks - "Ineffective Organizations using
Components Inefficiently" <G>

Jimmy
```

(And I'm wise to you being "with me", Jimmy. Might as well be on a different
Planet...<G>)


> What you are saying was written
> in a different sense by Gerry Garfunkel in his 'COBOL 85 Examples'. To
> paraphrase, "Users would be 'empowered' by pre written software, such as
> spreadsheets DBs etc.".
>
