```
"MBS" <salviejo@plaind.com> wrote:
> Does multithreading exist in COBOL?
>
> Anyone have an example?

Here's a "whitepaper" that outlines Micro Focus COBOL support for
multi-threading (examples included):

COBOL Multi-Threading
Author: Barry Rosetti

Issue 1
February 1996

Copyright(c) 1996, 2002 Micro Focus, All Rights Reserved.

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Table of Contents

 1      What is  Multi-Threading?
            Multi-Threading and the Operating System
            Multi-Threading and the Application
 2      Synchronizing Execution and Resolving Contention
            Use of Program Attributes
            Use of Data Attributes
            Use of Synchronization Primitives
            Using a Mutex
            Using a Monitor
            Using a Semaphore
            Using an Event
 3      The Micro Focus COBOL RTS
 4      Writing a Multi-threaded Application
            Reentrant COBOL Programs
            Initialization of applications
            Manipulating Threads
            Thread handles
            Creating and terminating a thread
            Canceling a thread
            Suspending a thread
            Identifying a thread
            Other Language Threads
            Creating thread-local data
            Common Programming Pitfalls
            Busy Wait
            Deadlock
            Single Thread Deadlock and Writer Starvation
 5      Optimizations & Programming Tips
 6      Compiler Directives

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 1

What is Multi-Threading?
========================

Good question. When we refer to multi-threading we can be referring
to a whole variety of things. There are two major topics, though,
multi-threading from an operating system perspective and
multi-threading from an application perspective, the following
section will discuss the former, while the rest of this document will
deal with the latter.

Multi-Threading and the Operating System
----------------------------------------

First and foremost, we are referring to a facility provided by the
operating system which allows an application to create threads of
execution within a process (a single running application). But what
is a thread? Its best to step back and take a look at what a process
is and how the operating system handles multiple processes running at
the same time.

Most modern operating systems allow many different users to be
running programs (processes) at, what appears to be, the same time.
If the operating system is hosted by a hardware system with a single
Central Processing Unit (CPU) then these programs cannot, obviously,
be running at exactly the same time - it just appears that way to
you. The operating system is responsible for allocating the CPU and
other system resources to the programs that need them and
(preemptively) switching these resources from one running program to
another 'every now and then'. This 'every now and then' is what gives
the appearance that the programs/processes are running at the same
time.

In a very similar way, many modern operating systems allow multiple
threads to exist within each of these individual processes. Again,
the operating system handles the allocation of the CPU and various
resources to individual threads and switches these allocations from
one running thread to another 'every now and then'. Again, this gives
the appearance that the threads are running at the same time.

So processes and threads get similar treatment from the operating
system. What distinguishes them from each other? The major thing is
the nature of the resources shared between processes running within
the Operating System and those shared between threads running within
a single process. All processes have distinct address spaces, open
files and other system resources - this allows each process to run
with minimal or no effect on other processes running at the same
time. So a running copy of the program Solitaire will have no effect
on a word processor or a data-base server running on the same
machine. This separation of resources has a definite run-time cost -
firstly, each process consumes system memory for its address space
and, secondly, the operating system must update a lot of internal
information whenever it switches execution context from one process
to another.

On the other hand, because threads run within a single process, all
of the process' threads share the same data and code address space,
the same open files and most other resources. Each thread does have
its own register set and stack space. So, overall, creation of a
thread does not require extensive system memory and when the
operating system finds it has to switch execution context from one
thread to another within the same process, about the only thing the
operating system needs to do is switch CPU ownership and register
sets. This is the reason that threads are often called 'light-weight
processes'.

Multi-Threading and the Application
-----------------------------------

A multi-threaded application is an application whose architecture
takes advantage of the multi-threading provided by the operating
system. Usually, these applications assigns specific jobs to
individual threads within the process and the threads communicate,
through various means, to synchronize their actions. For example, a
GUI data processing application might be architected such that the
User Interface is completely handled by a single thread, while the
actual work of the application is performed by another thread. This
architecture would allow the complete separation of the business
logic from the user interface logic within the application.

Another interesting use for multi-threading within an application is
in the server side of a client-server application. The server could
be designed so that for each service request submitted to a main
controller/communications thread, a separate service thread is
created to process that request and communicate the results back to
the controller. The controller would then communicate these results
back to the client. The main benefit here is that the server is not
tied up acting on one client's request to the extent that it cannot
respond to other client's requests quickly.

Of course, client-server applications that have immediate (or very
fast) server response have been written without multi-threading for
years. This is usually accomplished by a main
controller/communications process creating separate service processes
for each client request. So why does an application like this need
multi-threading? The main answer is: Efficient use of system
resources. As was discussed before, the creation of a thread requires
very little operating system resources (memory, context swap overhead
and so on) and the sharing of files and other resources is very easy.
The creation of a process requires more system resources and it tends
to be much more difficult to communicate and share files between
processes. So, multi-threading in applications such as this allows
you to make the best use out of your existing, limited, hardware
resources and also allows simple resource sharing.

There is a down-side to all this though. Each thread in a
multi-threaded application must be (painfully) aware of the resources
that it might be sharing with other threads in the same process. The
programmer must remember that threads execute at the same time and
that two threads writing to the same data item, without any form of
execution synchronization, will likely result in the corruption of
the data item.

For example, consider the following code fragments and their possible
order of execution:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  group-item.
            05  field-1    pic x.
            05  field-2    pic x.

        *> Thread 1 execution           *> Thread 2 execution

        MOVE 'A'  TO field-1 *>1st      MOVE 'C'  TO field-2 *>2nd

        MOVE 'B'  TO field-2 *>3rd      MOVE 'D'  TO field-1 *>4th

        DISPLAY group-item   *>5th      DISPLAY group-item   *>6th

In this case, neither thread will display their 'expected' results.
While Thread 1 expected to display 'AB' and Thread 2 expected to
display 'DC', both threads will display 'DB'. So neither thread
achieved their intended results. This is where the best part of
multi-threaded programming comes into play. That is synchronization
of execution between threads and avoiding/resolving data contention
between threads.

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 2

Synchronizing Execution and Resolving Contention
================================================

Uncontrolled multi-threading within an application can cause
unpredictable end results. There are a variety of ways to synchronize
execution and resolve data contention between threads that help
achieve predictable application results. The methods you use rely on
the characteristics of your application's data access and the
requirements for true multi-threading within the various application
modules (the individual COBOL programs).

The following sections outline some of the techniques that can be
used within your application to achieve predictable results. These
techniques include assigning each program and data item one of the
various multi-threading attributes and using the various
synchronization primitives.

Use of Program Attributes
-------------------------

There are three main program attributes that affect multi-threading
within a COBOL program. These are attributes are assigned by
including or omitting one of several compiler directives on program
compilation. The directives used affect the allocation of the
program's system work areas and can cause automatic system locking of
the compiled program.

By default, the first program attribute provides no provision for
multi-threading and is achieved by specifying no special compiler
directives. This option causes system work areas to be allocated
statically and so they are subject to contention. This approach has
several advantages, call speed and stack usage are among them, but it
is up to the user application to make absolutely sure that only one
thread executes within a non-multithreading program at a time. This
can be accomplished through implicit program logic in any calling
program, for example, when the application, by design, has only one
thread calling a non-multi-threaded program. Alternatively, one of
the synchronization primitives (such as a Mutex) can be locked in the
calling program just before the called program is entered and then
unlocked when the called program has returned.

The second attribute is assigned by specifying the SERIAL compiler
directive. This directive causes system work areas to be allocated
statically but also causes the program to be locked on entry and
unlocked on exit. This ends up allowing only one thread to execute
the program at a given time and so eliminates any contention on
system or user work areas. No other explicit application logic is
required. This technique has the advantage of allowing traditional
COBOL programs to be included in a multi-threaded application without
any source changes. One of its major disadvantages, though, is the
limiting of the level of multi-threading within the application.
Ideally, a multi-threaded application should allow free execution of
as much code as possible to allow maximum performance.

Another major disadvantage to the SERIAL attribute is the run-time
cost of locking and unlocking the program - this can significantly
affect call speed and overall application performance. For this
reason, as few modules as possible should be compiled with this
directive and heavy reliance should be placed on specifying no
multi-threading attribute for called programs. For example, if
program A must be compiled with the SERIAL attribute and program A is
the only program in the application that calls program B and C, then
by application design, program B and C are already serialized and can
be compiled with no multi-threading attribute (as discussed above).

The final attribute is given by the REENTRANT compiler directive.
This directive causes all system work areas to be allocated
dynamically on the stack (eliminating thread contention on these
areas) and allows multiple threads to enter the program at the same
time. This is the directive that the programmer would want to use for
most (if not all) of the modules in a multi-threaded application. The
only problem with reentrant programs is that the program itself must
resolve all possible data contention on its WORKING-STORAGE and FILE
SECTION. One or more of the other techniques for resolving data
contention must be used to accomplish this.

Use of Data Attributes
----------------------

The attributes of a data item determines if there is possibility for
contention on that data item amongst the threads that access it.
There is never a possibility for thread contention on data items
defined in the LOCAL-STORAGE or THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE SECTIONs or on
data items defined with the THREAD-LOCAL data attribute. Any other
data items defined within the program are shared between threads and
so there is possibility for contention. This includes WORKING-STORAGE
items, FILE SECTION items, some compiler special registers (except
the RETURN-CODE register) and other various implicitly defined data
items such as file status fields.

Data defined in the LOCAL-STORAGE section is traditionally used in
recursive COBOL programs, for every recursion of the program a new
instance of this data is allocated on the stack. Since each thread in
a multi-threaded application has its own stack, this attribute is
ideal for defining contention-free temporary and work items within
reentrant programs. By design, a reentrant COBOL program also allows
recursion within a single thread.

There are two disadvantages to using data defined in the
LOCAL-STORAGE SECTION. Firstly, it can't be initialized with value
clauses (any value clause is treated as documentation only) and,
secondly, it disappears when the reentrant/recursive program exits.
After an exit, on the next entry into the program, the LOCAL-STORAGE
field values will have undefined values. So if a thread requires the
program to preserve state across calls, some other mechanism must be
used.

The THREAD-LOCAL attribute and the THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section
resolve these problems. THREAD-LOCAL data is unique to each thread,
is persistent across calls and can be initialized with value clauses.
A data item defined as THREAD-LOCAL can be viewed as a
thread-specific WORKING-STORAGE data item. This kind of data is very
useful for resolving contention problems in most reentrant programs.
In many cases, a non-file-handling program can be made completely
reentrant by simply changing the WORKING-STORAGE section header with
a THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section header. The programmer can fine-tune
data allocation by defining all read-only constants in
WORKING-STORAGE and all read-write data items as THREAD-LOCAL.

There are some disadvantages to the use of THREAD-LOCAL storage
though. Firstly, it does not allow easy communication of data values
between threads, WORKING-STORAGE with appropriate thread
synchronization should be used for this. Secondly, its use can add
quite a bit of an overhead to program calls - if it is used in a
high-traffic module, then overall application performance can be
seriously affected. Finally, it is not possible to have EXTERNAL
items defined as THREAD-LOCAL. Currently, if thread-local data must
be shared between application modules, the CBL_TSTORE_ API must be
used (more details on this in section Creating thread-local data on
page 1).

Of course, sometimes threads require more than just private data.
Most multi-threaded applications communicate and coordinate thread
execution through shared data that is accessed by each thread under a
strict protocol. In COBOL this is accomplished through the use of
WORKING-STORAGE or FILE SECTION data in conjunction with the use of
various synchronization primitives that resolve any possibility for
destructive contention on the data.

Use of Synchronization Primitives
---------------------------------

Execution synchronization of threads accessing shared data is
critical for predictable results in a multi-threaded application.
Understanding the nature of the various data accesses between threads
is the first step in determining what synchronization primitives and
regimes should be used. Once the data access has been characterized,
it is a simple matter to follow a synchronization regime for data
items among all threads. The following sections outline common data
sharing problems and their solutions.

Using a Mutex
-------------

The simplest form of data sharing problem is when multiple threads
require mutually exclusive access to shared data at some point during
their processing. The area of code that accesses this shared data is
called a critical section and these critical sections can be
protected by the use of a Mutex which is logically associated with
the shared data items (the name Mutex comes from Mutual Exclusion).
The Mutex associated with the shared data items is locked before a
critical section is entered and unlocked when that critical section
is finished.

It is vital that all threads lock the protecting Mutex before
accessing any of the data that is being protected. If even one thread
fails to follow this regime, then unpredictable application results
could ensue.

The following code illustrates the protection of two critical
sections that access a table, adding to it or counting items in it.
The working storage data items table-xxx are protected by
table-mutex.

        Working-Storage Section.
        78  table-length       value 20.
        01  table-mutex        usage mutex-pointer.
        01  table-current      pic x(4) comp-x value 0.
        01  table-1.
            05  table-item-1   pic x(10) occurs 20.

        Local-Storage Section.
        01  table-count        pic x(4) comp-x.
        01  table-i            pic x(4) comp-x.

            *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            move 0                           to table-current
            open table-mutex

            *> Add an item to table-1, this is a critical section
            set table-mutex        to on
            if table-current < table-length
                add 1              to table-current
                move 'filled in'   to table-item-1(table-current)
            end-if
            set table-mutex        to off

            *> Count items in table-1, this is a critical section
            set table-mutex        to on
            move 0 to table-count
            perform varying table-i from 1 by 1 until
              table-i > table-current
                if  table-item-1(table-i) = 'filled in'
                     add 1 to table-count
                end-if
            end-perform
            set table-mutex        to off

It is worth noting that, in the last example, the Mutex was
definitely required to prevent a thread from adding to the table and
another thread from looking at the table at the same time. The only
problem is that in using a Mutex we are severely limiting the level
of multi-threading within the application. Ideally, in order to
maximize multi-threading in applications whose data access is similar
to this, multiple threads would be able to count items in the table
simaltaneously, but no counters or other adders would be allowed
access to critical sections while one thread is already adding to the
table.

This illustrates a case of the Readers and Writers synchronization
problem, which has been written about extensively in operating system
text books. A solution to this problem would be given by a
synchronization primitive that allows multiple threads to be active
in read-only critical sections but prevents any other access when a
thread is active in a writer critical section.

Using a Monitor
---------------

Monitors provide a clean solution to the Readers and Writers Problem.
Basically, they are used by having the critical section 'declaring'
what type of data access the critical section will be performing on
the protected data i.e. reading or writing (or browsing, but we'll
get into that later). The following code is an example of a monitor
being used to allow multiple counters on the table but disallowing
any adder when a counter is active and allowing one adder but
disallowing any counters (readers) when that adder is active.

        Working-Storage Section.
        78  table-length       value 20.
        01  table-monitor      usage monitor-pointer.
        01  table-current      pic x(4) comp-x value 0.
        01  table-1.
            05  table-item-1   pic x(10) occurs table-length.

        Local-Storage Section.
        01  table-count        pic x(4) comp-x.
        01  table-i            pic x(4) comp-x.

            *> Initialization code, executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            move 0                            to table-current
            open table-monitor

            *> Add an item to table-1, this is a writer critical
            *> section
            set table-monitor                 to writing
            if table-current < table-length
                add 1              to table-current
                move 'filled in'   to table-item-1(table-current)
            end-if
            set table-monitor                 to not writing

            *> Count items in table-1, this is a reader critical
            *> section
            set table-monitor                 to reading
            move 0 to table-count
            perform varying table-i from 1 by 1
              until table-i > table-current
                if  table-item-1(table-i) = 'filled in'
                    add 1 to table-count
                end-if
            end-perform
            set table-monitor                 to not reading

There is a fairly straightforward extension to the classical monitor
synchronization facility that can be very useful in real world
applications. This is the provision of a 'browser' critical section.
A browser is basically a reader that, after an initial scan to see if
conditions are right, may (or may not) want to write to the protected
data items. While a browser is active, any number of readers are
allowed in their critical sections but no other browsers or writers
are allowed. If the browser thread determines that it needs to write
to the protected data, it requests a conversion of the browse lock to
a write lock. The conversion process waits until all readers are
finished (not allowing any more readers or writers) and then allows
the browser to proceed as a writer with exclusive access to the
protected data items (whose state is guaranteed to be the same as it
was when the browser was just looking at the protected data). The
following is a very simple example of a browser critical section:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  data-monitor      usage monitor-pointer.
        01  data-value        pic x(4) comp-x value 0.

            *> Initialization code, executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            open data-monitor

            *> Add an item to table-1, this is a browser critical
            *> section
            set data-monitor        to browsing
            if data-value < 20
                set data-monitor    to writing
                                    converting from browsing
                add 5               to data-value
                set data-moniotr    to not writing
            else
                set data-moniotr    to not browsing
            end-if

Note that we do NOT recommend that you use a browse lock for such a
simple check. Usually, a browser would be used only if a significant
amount of work has to be done to determine if a write lock is
actually required and you want to maximize multi-threading throughout
your application. There are various other monitor conversions
available to help the program maximize the level of multi-threading
in his application - please see the API reference in the appendices
to this document.

Using a Semaphore
-----------------

One of the meanings of semaphore in real life terms is a gate that
lowers to prevent passage over a level-crossing and raises to allow
passage through that same crossing. In software terms it is a
synchronization object that, in simple usages, acts as a gate which
lowers to prevent passage through code and raises to allow passage
(of another thread) through that code. This semantic is very much
like a Mutex. In fact a Semaphore can be used instead of a Mutex.

The following code illustrates this:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  data-semaphore     usage semaphore-pointer.
        01  data-value         pic x(4) comp-x value 0.

        *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded mode
        open data-semaphore
        set data-semaphore  up by 1    *> Initialize to raised

        *> Add change data-value, this is a critical section
        set data-semaphore  down by 1
        add 1 to data-value
        *> Allow other thread to pass semaphore
        set data-semaphore  up by 1

Note that just after the OPEN verb, the Semaphore is raised by 1.
This allows the first acquire to succeed but any following acquires
to be blocked until the Semaphore is released again.

So why provide both Semaphores and Mutexes? Well, while a Semaphore
is less efficient than a Mutex, it is more flexible: one thread can
simply release (or raise) it while any other thread can then acquire
(or lower) it. Contrast this with a Mutex which must always be
acquired before it can be released and these operations must happen
within the same thread. So Semaphores provide a way of signaling from
one thread to another, much like another kind of real world
Semaphore, the flag Semaphore.

The following code sample uses two different Semaphores to establish
handshaking between two threads. This handshaking allows one thread
to signal the production of a new data value and the other thread to
signal the corresponding consumption of that data value:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  produced-semaphore     usage semaphore-pointer.
        01  data-value             pic x(4) comp-x value 0.
        01  consumed-semaphore     usage semaphore-pointer.

            *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            open produced-semaphore
            open consumed-semaphore
            set consumed-semaphore      up by 1

            *> This code is executed once to produce a data value
            set consumed-semaphore    down by 1
            add 10  to data-value
            *> Signal data value changed
            set produced-semaphore      up by 1

            *> Another thread, waiting for the data-value to change,
            *> executes this code once as well.
            Set produced-semaphore     down by 1
            display data-value
            *> Signal data value used
            set consumed-semaphore       up by 1

The above code illustrates another common synchronization problem -
the Producer-Consumer Problem. The simplest Producer-Consumer Problem
asks, 'Given that there is one producer of data and one consumer of
that data, how do you synchronize execution between the producer and
consumer such that when the consumer is awake he always has data to
operate on?'. Semaphores, as used above, solve this problem.

Further, these Semaphores also count the number of releases/raises
that are outstanding on a Semaphore and will allow that many acquires
to pass unblocked. This kind of counting Semaphore allows the
producer to produce multiple data values (presumably in an array)
before blocking to wait for the consumer to catch up.

The following code illustrates a simple example of a
producer/consumer pair where the producer is allowed to create data
values until the data table cannot handle any more - at which point
he blocks until some values are consumed:

        Working-Storage Section.
        78  table-size             value 20.
        01  produced-semaphore     usage semaphore-pointer.
        01  filler.
            05  table-data-value   pic x(4) comp-x
                                   occurs table-size times value 0.
        01  consumed-semaphore     usage semaphore-pointer.

        Local-Storage Section.
        01  table-i                pic x(4) comp-x.

            *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            open produced-semaphore
            open consumed-semaphore
            *> start raised 20 times
            set consumed-semaphore up by table-size

            *> Producer thread
            move 1              to table-i
            perform until 1 = 0
                set consumed-semaphore  down by 1
                add table-i             to table-data-value(table-i)
                set produced-semaphore    up by 1
                add 1         to table-i
                if  table-i > table-size
                    move 1    to table-i
                end-if
            end-perform.

            *> Consumer thread
            move 1              to table-i
            perform until 1 = 0
                set produced-semaphore   down by 1
                display 'Current produced value is'
                  table-data-value(table-i)
                set consumed-semaphore     up by 1
                add 1         to table-i
                if  table-i > table-size
                    move 1    to table-i
                end-if
            end-perform.

Using an Event
--------------

An event is similar to a Semaphore in that it allows one thread to
signal another that something has happened that requires attention.
For several reasons it is more flexible and slightly more complex.
One reason is that an event, once posted, must be explicitly cleared.

The following is an example of the Producer-Consumer code using Event
synchronization instead of Semaphore synchronization:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  produced-event     usage event-pointer.
        01  data-value         pic x(4) comp-x value 0.
        01  consumed-event     usage event-pointer.

            *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            open produced-event
            open consumed-event

            *> Initialize as 'posted'
            set consumed-event   to on

            *> protocol for the producer side...
            wait for consumed-event

            *> Clear event
            set consumed-event   to false
            add 10  to data-value

            *> Signal that data value has changed
            set produced-event   to true      *> Post event

            *> protocol for the consumer side, wait for the
            *> data-value to change
            wait for produced-event
            set produced-event   to false     *> Clear event
            display data-value
            *> Signal other thread that he can proceed we got the
            *> data-value
            set consumed-event   to true      *> Post event

If there are only two threads (the producer and consumer threads)
executing the above code, then everything will work fine. If another
thread comes in and starts executing as a producer or consumer,
interesting (i.e. unwanted) things will happen. This is because an
event once posted will wake up all threads waiting on that event,
unlike a Semaphore which allows only one thread to pass after a
release/raise. After the event has been posted and the waiting
threads woken, it is necessary for each of the woken threads to
determine if it should take some form of action on the event
(including clearing the event).

The last point can make events difficult to work with when there are
multiple waiters but it also allows the building of custom
synchronization objects for special needs.

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 3

The Micro Focus COBOL RTS
=========================

The previous chapters have attempted to illustrate the difficulties
and costs (on a per-thread basis) of writing a multi-threaded
application. Most of this difficulty and cost comes from the need to
synchronize the various threads' execution and resolve data
contention issues. While, the flexibility and power of a
multi-threaded application far outweighs these costs, if you do not
need to create a multi-threaded application, you can program
traditionally and should not incur any extra run-time costs in your
application. There are some specific steps necessary to achieve this
end though, as detailed below.

The multi-threaded Run-Time System which supports Micro Focus COBOL
applications must be able to handle both multi-threaded and
single-threaded applications. As a result, the multi-threaded RTS
incurs many of the synchronization costs that a standard
multi-threaded COBOL application would. Further, most of these costs
are incurred regardless of the kind of application being run, either
multi-threaded or single-threaded. Because of this, and in order to
allow single-threaded applications to run at maximum speed, we have
provided two separate run-time systems, a multi-threaded RTS, which
supports all applications, and a single-threaded RTS which supports
only single-threaded applications.

You may chose between these run-time systems at application run time
by the trigger name or at CBLLINK time by the use of an option, as
detailed in the following tables.

The following table details the run trigger names and the various
COBOL RTS DLLs that will be used when the application is run. Note
that the listing of all possibilities of run-triggers, including the
diagnostic RTS triggers is included:

Trigger Names   Description           DLL Names
---------------------------------------------------------------------
RUN, RUNS,      Single-threaded RTS   CBLRTSS, CBLVIOS, CBLINTS,
RUNW, RUNSW                           CBLANMS, CBLIFNS, CBLFHS,
                                      COB32API
RUNM, RUNMW     Multi-threaded RTS    CBLRTSM, CBLVIOM, CBLINTM,
                                      CBLANMM, CBLIFNM, CBLFHM,
                                      COB32API

The following table details the import libraries used when CBLLINK
creates a system executable for use with the dynamically loaded RTS:

Cbllink Option  Description                    Import Library Names
---------------------------------------------------------------------
<none>          Dynamic, Single-threaded RTS   CBLRTSSI.LIB
-J              Dynamic, Multi-threaded RTS    CBLRTSMI.LIB

The following table details the static link libraries used when
creating a statically linked COBOL application:

Cbllink Option  Description                    Static Link Lib Names
---------------------------------------------------------------------
-t              Static, Single-threaded RTS    CBLRTSS.LIB
-t -J           Static, Multi-threaded RTS     CBLRTSM.LIB


-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 4

Writing a Multi-threaded Application
====================================

Reentrant COBOL Programs
------------------------

The first thing to consider when writing a multi-threaded application
is how each module should operate. Should the module be compiled as a
standard COBOL program, should it be compiled as a SERIAL program or
should it be compiled as a REENTRANT module? In the section Use of
Program Attributes on page 1 we gave an overview of the usages of
standard COBOL compilation, SERIAL compilation and REENTRANT
compilation. Out of the three, reentrant programs need more attention
(in addition to the cautions of protecting user-defined static data
such as WORKING-STORAGE).

First, not every program can be compiled as reentrant. Use of some
COBOL features will preclude reentrant compilation, but, thankfully,
most of these COBOL features are obsolete or archaic in ANSI Standard
COBOL and their use should be discontinued. Programs that use the
following features cannot be compiled with the REENTRANT directive:

    o  the ALTER statement
    o  the ON statement
    o  the COBOL DEBUG facility
    o  PROCEDURE DIVISION overlays - note this can be overcome by
       using either the NOSEG or NOOVL directives
    o  the IS INITIAL attribute in the PROGRAM-ID
    o  the STICKY-LINKAGE compiler directive
    o  the STICKY-PERFORM compiler directive

The following restrictions and notes on the use of reentrant programs
and multi-threading within those programs also apply:

    o  Most file handling verbs will need explicit user protection
       with one of the execution synchronization primitives discussed
       earlier. Internally, the file handler is thread-safe, however
       within the user program file records, buffers and status
       fields are set without the benefit of locking - this can lead
       to contention and resulting corruption of these fields if no
       user measures are taken.
    o  Execution of any file operation is serialized within the
       file-handler with a global file-handling Mutex. This means
       that only one thread may access any file at a given time. This
       could lead to problems if a read from a file is delayed for
       some reason (e.g. because of a record lock), in this case no
       other thread will be able to do any file handling operations
       until the delayed read is finished.
    o  Execution of any sort operations are serialized within the
       sort handler with the global file-handling Mutex. While a sort
       is on-going no other thread will be able to perform other sort
       or file handling operations. Execution of the INPUT and OUTPUT
       procedures occur in the thread that started the sort
       operation. File handling within these procedures will execute
       normally.
    o  Execution of the DISPLAY verb will require user protection
       with one of the synchronization primitives discussed earlier.
       Internally, the console display handler is thread-safe,
       however each complex display is broken down into multiple
       calls to the display handler - this can lead to interleaved
       displays of data items. In general, it is best to assign only
       one thread to console I/O within a multi-threaded application.
    o  Nested programs may not be the starting point of a newly
       created thread. Only ENTRY points, outermost programs in a
       compilation unit and other-language external entry points may
       be the starting point for thread creation.
    o  Execution of the ACCEPT verb is serialized within the accept
       handler. This means that all other threads are prevented from
       doing any ACCEPT or DISPLAY operations while the original
       ACCEPT is outstanding.

The following performance and resource penalties are incurred in
reentrant COBOL programs within a multi-threaded application:

    o  Use of the THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section, on some platforms,
       can incur significant program entry costs. If possible, use
       LOCAL-STORAGE instead for thread-local work variables.
    o  A reentrant program will consume more stack space than either
       a standard or a serialized COBOL program. This is because
       system work-areas must be allocated on the stack, instead of
       statically.

Initialization of applications
------------------------------

The initialization of multi-threaded applications needs a bit of
care. First, as good programming practice, the application should
determine if it is running with an RTS that supports multi-threading.
This is easily done with the following code:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  thread-id          usage pointer.

        *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded mode
        *> to check if the RTS supports multi-threading or the
        *> CBL_THREAD_ API.
        call 'CBL_THREAD_SELF' using thread-id
        on exception
             *> No cbl_thread api support
        end-call
        if  return-code == 1008
             *> Running in a single-threaded rts
        end-if

Note that this last code sample makes use of a CBL_ API call for it's
function. While many of the facilities provided by the RTS for
multi-threaded programming are accessable through Micro Focus COBOL
syntax, all can be coded directly through the use of general API
calls. Further, to make use of some of the more advanced
multi-threaded programming functionality, API calls must be used
directly.

After it is determined that the RTS supports multi-threading, all
synchronization objects should be be initialized using the
appropriate OPEN syntax. It is easiest to do this as part of the main
program, before any other threads are created within the application.
However, if by application design or because of modularity or
mixed-language considerations, this is not possible, we have provided
a single pre-initialized Mutex per program. This Mutex is accessed
using CBL_THREAD_PROG_LOCK and CBL_THREAD_PROG_UNLOCK API calls.
Using these calls, it is possible to ensure that any handles for
program-local synchronization primitives are initialized once (and
only once) during the execution of the application. The following
code illustrates a sample use of these API calls for program
initialization while running in multi-threaded mode:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  first-flag            pic x comp-x value 1.
            88  first-time        value 1.
            88  not-first-time    value 0.

        *> Initialization code executed while in multi-threaded mode
        *> Ensures that program local data is initialized properly.

        if  first-time then
            call 'CBL_THREAD_PROG_LOCK'
            if  first-time then
                *> Initialize program local data and synchronization
                *> objects
                ...
                set not-first-time     to true
            end-if
            call 'CBL_THREAD_PROG_UNLOCK'
        end-if

Note the double checking of the first-time level 88. This is a good
optimization technique in multi-threaded applications. The idea of
the optimization is to avoid the overhead of locking a Mutex if we
know that our intended action has already been performed. In this
case, if multiple threads enter the program before it is properly
initialized, they will all find that first-time is true and so issue
a call to CBL_THREAD_PROG_LOCK, but only one will acquire the lock
while the first-time is still true. This is the thread that will do
the initialization.

After the initialization is completed, and while the initializing
thread still owns the lock, the first-time flag is set to false. All
successive threads acquiring the lock will see that the
initialization has already been done, as first-time is now false and
unlock the program immediately. Any following threads that enter
after initialization will also see the first-time flag is false and
will not attempt to acquire the program lock (and so reduce program
entry overhead).

Note that in order for this optimization to work properly on all
platforms, the first time in flag should as simple as possible (i.e.
a single byte data item).

Manipulating Threads
--------------------

The multi-threaded RTS supports threads created by the RTS itself,
via the START verb or the CBL_THREAD_ API, and also other-language
threads created directly by the operating system. Since COBOL syntax
and the CBL_THREAD_ API are portable, these are the preferred methods
of thread manipulation. Further, the CBL_THREAD_ API can also be
called from other language modules, allowing any multi-threaded
application to take full advantage of the advanced facilities
provided by the RTS.

Thread handles
--------------

In general, threads are identified to the RTS with thread handles.
Thread handles are provided to creating threads as return values from
the START verb or the CBL_THREAD_CREATE API call or to an individual
thread by the return value from CBL_THREAD_SELF API call. This handle
uniquely identifies the thread to the various multi-threading COBOL
verbs and the CBL_THREAD_ API calls and, with reasonable care, can be
used by any of the active threads in the run-unit not just the
creator of a thread. It is important to remember that the lifetime of
a thread and that of its thread handle might not be the same.
Depending on how a thread was created and what operations have been
done on it, when a thread terminates, its handle could still be
valid. In order to allow the system to recover the resources
associated with the handle, it is up to the user to explicitly detach
the handle for a thread.

A handle can become detached in one of several ways. When a thread is
created with the START verb or the CBL_THREAD_CREATE API call, its
handle is, by default, created as detached. When an other-language
thread becomes known to the RTS, its handle is automatically and
always created as detached. A non-detached handle can be detached
with a call to CBL_THREAD_DETACH. In any case, a detached handle must
always be used with care, as when the thread terminates the handle
immediately becomes invalid. If a thread with a non-detached handle
has already terminated, then a call to CBL_THREAD_DETACH with that
thread handle will immediately cause the handle to become invalid.

A non-detached handle is useful for retrieval of return values (via
the WAIT verb or the CBL_THREAD_WAIT) API call and for inspection of
thread identification data (via the CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_GET API call)
after a thread has potentially terminated. The START verb provides a
way to identify a newly created thread by returning a non-detached
thread handle and the CBL_THREAD_CREATE API call provides a flag to
indicate that the thread should be created and a non-detached handle
should be returned.

Creating and terminating a thread
---------------------------------

Threads are created by the START verb, the CBL_THREAD_CREATE API call
or with the specific operating system call for thread creation.
Threads created by the first two of these methods are called COBOL
Threads, threads created directly by the specific operating system
facility are called Other Language Threads. Again, as the creation
and manipulation of COBOL Threads is portable and preferred, we will
only deal with them in the following examples.

The following complete application illustrates simple thread
creation:

       $set reentrant
        Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
        01  thread-handle                   usage thread-pointer.
        01  thread-return                   usage pointer.

        Linkage Section.
        01  thread-parm                     picture x(32).
        01  thread-return-record            picture x(32).

        Procedure Division.
        Start 'CREATED' using 'This is a 32 character parameter'
            identified by thread-handle
        on exception
            stop run
        end-start
        wait for thread-handle  returning into thread-return
        set address of thread-return-record to thread-return
        display thread-return-record

        stop run.

        Entry "CREATED" using thread-parm.
        display thread-parm
        stop run returning address of thread-parm.

This application simply creates a thread with the starting point
"CREATED". The "CREATED" thread displays its parameter and returns
the address of that parameter for use in the parent thread. Note
that:

    o  The starting point for a thread must be either a non-nested
       PROGRAM-ID, a COBOL ENTRY point or an other-language (e.g. C)
       externally visible routine. It must not be a nested program, a
       section or paragraph name.
    o  The starting point name is specified as a text string, the
       overhead in using this text string to find the entry point is
       equivalent to resolving the verb CALL identifier. It is
       possible to avoid this overhead by using a procedure pointer
       as the object of the START verb.
    o  The starting point for every created thread is provided with
       only one parameter. If the BY CONTENT phrase is used in
       passing this parameter a copy of it is made by the system
       before the thread is created, leaving the calling thread free
       to modify the original parameter upon return from the START
       verb.
    o  Creation of a thread with a non-detached handle (via the
       IDENTIFIED BY clause) allows the return value to be obtained
       later by the WAIT verb. Upon completion of the WAIT verb the
       specified thread handle becomes invalid and all resources
       associated with the subject thread are released.
    o  The STOP RUN RETURNING in the "CREATED" thread does not end
       the run-unit, instead it simply provides a return value and
       terminates the thread. It is equivalent to:

           call 'CBL_THREAD_EXIT' using by value
             address of thread-parm.

    o  STOP RUN in an Other Language Thread threads or the main
       thread of a Run-unit will wait for all active COBOL Threads to
       finish and then terminate the run-unit.
    o  Return values from threads are always pointers. This allows
       you to return both simple and complex data structures.

As was mentioned above, a thread can terminate itself with STOP RUN
or a call to CBL_THREAD_EXIT. A thread also terminates normally when
the starting point program exits via EXIT PROGRAM or GOBACK. It is
also sometimes useful for another thread to cause a COBOL Thread to
be terminated. The CBL_THREAD_KILL API call achieves this.

Canceling a thread
------------------

Threads are canceled with the CBL_THREAD_KILL API call. This call
causes immediate, abnormal termination of a COBOL Thread thread but,
in general, it should not be used as part of general application
thread control. The main reason for this is that some user and system
synchronization resources will not be properly unlocked and/or freed
upon thread termination. This can affect the synchronization within
the user application and the RTS, very likely leading to serious
problems with the application run.

CBL_THREAD_KILL can reasonably be used within a critical error
handler in the main thread. In this error handler, thread handles can
be acquired by way of the CBL_THREAD_LIST_ API, all threads can be
canceled and the application can then exit via STOP RUN. This is less
dangerous than the random usage of CBL_THREAD_KILL as it minimizes
the chance of needing a locked synchronization resource, but there is
still a possibility of file corruption or deadlock during run-unit
termination.

In any case, most applications should avoid using CBL_THREAD_KILL.
This can be achieved by creating thread identification data (through
CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_ALLOC) that has a terminate flag in it. That data
can then be polled in each thread at a level where no locks are held.
If the terminate flag is set, then the polling thread can exit
gracefully. See the section Identifying a thread on page 1 for an
example of using the CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_ API.

Suspending a thread
-------------------

In many multi-threaded applications it is not uncommon for a thread
to 'run out of work' but not want to terminate just yet. In a
client-server architecture, for example, the main service thread
could be polling an input queue for requests and if it finds none
then it makes sense to allow another process or thread to have the
CPU before polling the input queue again. This is simply done by
calling CBL_THREAD_YIELD which yields the processor to some other
thread in the application (or some other thread in another process,
depending on the operating system).

Another possibility is that a thread needs to yield the CPU
indefinitely and obtain it only when some sort of event has
definitely happened. CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND allows suspension of the
calling thread until some other thread does a CBL_THREAD_RESUME using
the thread handle for the suspended thread. A thread can call
CBL_THREAD_RESUME one or more times before the targeted thread
CBL_THREAD_SUSPENDs itself and, in this case, the call to
CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND will return to the calling thread immediately and
not give up ownership of the CPU. From the discussions on
synchronization objects, you will see that this semantic is very
similar to that of a counting Semaphore, in fact the
Producer-Consumer problem can be solved using only the
CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND and CBL_THREAD_RESUME API calls.

Identifying a thread
--------------------

It is sometimes useful for a thread to distinguish itself from any
other created threads in an application. For example, if two threads
in a four thread application establish a producer-consumer
relationship, it can be useful for the two cooperating threads to
find out what each others thread handles are. Once these handles are
obtained, all synchronization can be done using only the
CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND and CBL_THREAD_RESUME calls. If each thread in the
application creates a name for itself (and the name relates its
functionality) and associates the name with its thread handle, then
the cooperating threads can scan the thread name list and, so, find
each other's handle.

Another possible use for associating globally accessible data to each
thread and its handle is to hold a termination flag, getting around
the possible need to use CBL_THREAD_KILL. Each thread in the
application can poll its termination flag to check for a termination
request. The terminating thread can then make sure no locks are held
and no synchronization actions are required before terminating
normally.

Globally accessible data for a thread is associated with the thread
handle by the CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_ALLOC call executed within the
thread. This data is retrieved by the CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_GET call, if
the thread handle is already known, or by the CBL_THREAD_LIST_ API
calls if the thread handle is not yet known. These mechanisms are
illustrated in the following sample application:

        **************************** MAINPROG.CBL *******************
        identification division.
        program-id. mainprog.

        Data Division.

        Local-Storage Section.
        01  iddata-ptr                    usage pointer.
        01  sub-iddata-ptr                usage pointer.
        01  sub-handle                    usage thread-pointer.

        Linkage Section.
        01  iddata-record.
            05  iddata-name               pic x(20).
            05  iddata-term               pic x comp-x value 0.

        Procedure Division.
        *>
        *> Establish identification data - don't provide
        *> initialization data when it is allocated, instead
        *> initialize it after the pointer is retrieved.
        *>
        call 'CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_ALLOC'    using by value zero
                                          length of iddata-record
        call 'CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_GET'      using iddata-ptr
                                          by value 0
        set address of iddata-record      to iddata-ptr
        move 'main'                       to iddata-name

        *>
        *> Create sub-thread
        *>
        start 'SUBPROG' identified by sub-handle

        *>
        *> Wait until child creates its iddata and then flag
        *> termination
        *>
        set sub-iddata-ptr                to NULL

        perform until 1 = 0
            call 'CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_GET'  using sub-iddata-ptr
                                          by value sub-handle
            if  sub-iddata-ptr not = null
                exit perform
            end-if
            call 'CBL_THREAD_YIELD'
        end-perform

        set address of iddata-record      to sub-iddata-ptr
        move 1                            to iddata-term

        *>
        *> Wait till the child resumes this thread
        *>
        call 'CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND'         using by value 0
        display 'All syncronization is complete on RTS termination'
        *> clear up thread's resources
        wait for sub-handle

        stop run.
        end program mainprog.

        ***************************** SUBPROG.CBL *******************
        identification division.
        program-id. subprog.

        Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
        01  sub-iddata.
            05  sub-name                  pic x(20) value 'sub'.
            05  sub-term                  pic x comp-x value 0.

        Local-Storage Section.
        01  iddata-ptr                    usage pointer.
        01  thread-handle                 usage pointer.
        01  thread-state                  pic x(4) comp-x.
        01  parent-handle                 usage pointer.

        Linkage Section.
        01  iddata-record.
            05  iddata-name               pic x(20).
            05  iddata-term               pic x comp-x value 0.

        Procedure Division.
        *>
        *> Establish identification data - provide initialization
        *> data
        *>
        call 'CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_ALLOC'    using sub-iddata
                             by value length of sub-iddata
        *>
        *> Find our parent thread and resume him
        *>
        call 'CBL_THREAD_LIST_START'      using thread-handle
                                          thread-state
                                          iddata-ptr
        set parent-handle                 to NULL

        perform until thread-handle = null or return-code not = 0
            if  iddata-ptr not = null
                set address of iddata-record   to iddata-ptr
                if  iddata-name = 'main'
                    set parent-handle          to thread-handle
                    exit perform
                end-if
            end-if
            call 'CBL_THREAD_LIST_NEXT'   using thread-handle
                                          thread-state
                                          iddata-ptr
        end-perform

        call 'CBL_THREAD_LIST_END'
        if  parent-handle = NULL
            display 'syncronization error'
            stop run
        end-if
        call 'CBL_THREAD_RESUME'          using by value
                                          parent-handle
        call 'CBL_THREAD_IDDATA_GET'      using iddata-ptr
                                          by value 0
        set address of iddata-record      to iddata-ptr
        perform until iddata-term = 1
            call 'CBL_THREAD_YIELD'
        end-perform

        exit program.
        end program subprog.

This rather lengthy example doesn't actually do anything but
establish handshaking for thread and application termination. Before
going into any discussion on it, it is worthwhile to note that this
kind of handshaking could have been accomplished more easily by
passing the main thread's handle into the child as a parameter. This
would have avoided the need to rely on identification data or to step
through the thread list.

First notice the two different ways of creating thread identification
data. The first way, in the parent thread, creates the data
uninitialized, retrieves the iddata pointer and then initializes the
area of memory that was allocated. The second, in the child, provides
initialization data to eliminate any possible execution window
between iddata allocation and iddata initialization by the
application. The method used in your application will depend on what
level of contention you expect on identification data.

Also, notice the loop in the parent that waits for the child to
create its thread identification data and the loop in the child that
waits for the parent to flag termination. The call to
CBL_THREAD_YIELD prevents these loops from being hard busy waits, but
would have been better coded using an event or condition
synchronization object or using CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND and
CBL_THREAD_RESUME. See the section Common Programming Pitfalls for
more discussion on busy waiting.

Finally, notice the use of the CBL_THREAD_LIST_ API. This API allows
a thread to step through all threads known to the RTS, obtaining the
thread handle, thread state and identification data pointer. In this
example we relied only on the handle and identifiction data pointer
but the state information can also be useful, as it lets the calling
thread know if the subject thread is detached, suspended or an
other-language thread.

It is important to realize that whenever the CBL_THREAD_LIST API is
used, the using thread has limitations on further CBL_THREAD_ calls
and all other threads are completely locked out from using any of
these calls as well as quite a few of the other RTS calls. For this
reason, it makes sense to keep the amount of code executed while
stepping the list to a minimum and certainly avoiding file or user
I/O while the list is locked. These restrictions are removed only
after the stepping is terminated by a call to CBL_THREAD_LIST_END.

Other Language Threads
----------------------

In a mixed language environment, threads created directly by
operating system facilities and not by the START verb or the
CBL_THREAD_CREATE API call have several restrictions and requirements
on them:

    o  They must be terminated by the approved operating system
       method of thread termination and not by any function in the
       CBL_THREAD_ API.
    o  Their handles are always created detached. This means that
       they cannot be waited on by the WAIT verb or the
       CBL_THREAD_WAIT API call and no return value is available from
       them.
    o  They must issue the call cobthreadtidy() or cobtidy() when the
       thread or the application, respectively, no longer needs COBOL
       RTS services. This allows the RTS to free memory and clean up
       thread state information.
    o  cobtidy(), cobexit() or the COBOL STOP RUN statement will
       cause the calling thread to be treated as the 'main' thread.
       As a result, the RTS will wait for all COBOL threads to
       terminate before any RTS cleanup processing is completed.

Note that all multi-threading functions supported by the RTS will
detect Other Language Thread handles and report invalid usages of
them as. It is, however, entirely up to the application to ensure
that RTS resources are freed for any thread that has executed any
COBOL or RTS code in the course of it's existence (as detailed
above).

Creating thread-local data
--------------------------

We have already discussed some of the ways of creating and using data
in multi-threaded applications in the section Use of Data Attributes
on page 1. These methods all created static data for direct reference
by the using program. It is however entirely reasonable for the
programmer to require dynamic data whose lifetime is that of the
creating thread or selective or external thread local data. These
facilities are provided by the CBL_ALLOC_THREAD_MEM and the
CBL_TSTORE_ APIs, respectively.

First, CBL_ALLOC_THREAD_MEM will allocate dynamic memory that is
automatically freed when the creating thread is canceled. This call
is ideal for creating thread local linked lists or other thread
specific operational structures. By default the memory allocated is
also attached to the allocating program, so if the program is
canceled then the memory is automatically freed (this default
semantic can feature can be disabled). Note that the dynamic memory
pointer returned by CBL_ALLOC_THREAD_MEM should probably be stored in
a THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section data item to protect its modification
by other threads.

Second, the CBL_TSTORE_ API allows the programmer to create a handle
for thread local storage and in any thread get a pointer to the
dynamic thread local data through this handle. When the handle is
created by CBL_TSTORE_CREATE, you provide the size of the thread
local data area that will be eventually created for each thread. The
data area is actually allocated only in threads that call
CBL_TSTORE_GET using the returned handle. All data allocated to a
thread is freed when the thread ends or, optionally, when the handle
creating program is canceled. It is possible to free all data
associated with a handle and the handle itself by using the
CBL_TSTORE_CLOSE call.

A CBL_TSTORE_ handle is usually stored in a WORKING-STORAGE data item
so that it is accessible to all threads requiring it. This data item
can also be EXTERNAL, so multiple programs can access the handle and,
within the same thread, have access to the same thread local data
area. The pointer to the thread local data returned by CBL_TSTORE_GET
should be stored in a thread-safe data item, i.e. one defined either
in THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE or LOCAL-STORAGE.

The following code fragment shows the use of the CBL_TSTORE_ API
calls:

        Working-Storage Section.
        01  tstore-handle      usage pointer external.
        Local-Storage Section.
        01  tstore-ptr         usage pointer.
        Linkage Section.
        01  tstore-record.
            05  tstore-field      pic x(20).

            *> Initialization code executed while in single threaded
            *> mode
            call 'CBL_TSTORE_CREATE' using tstore-handle
                                     by value length of tstore-record
                                     zero *> Associated with creating
                                          *> program
            ... Code omitted to create a few threads ...
            stop run

            *> Code executed in possibly many threads.

            *> If we need it then get the thread local storage area -
            *> threads that don't need this data won't have it
            *> allocated and won't incur the overhead of the tstore
            *> get call.

            if  some-condition
                call 'CBL_TSTORE_GET' using  by value tstore-handle
                                             by reference tstore-ptr
                set address of tstore-record to tstore-ptr
                move 'some thread data'      to tstore-record
            end-if

Common Programming Pitfalls
---------------------------

It is worthwhile to discuss a few of the common problems a
multi-threaded application can encounter while trying to syncronize
thread execution. These are outlined in the following sections.

Busy Wait
---------

The first of these is the use of a busy wait for synchronization, as
illustrated in the following code fragment:

        *> Code executed by one thread
        perform until done = 1
            *> Do nothing - we are waiting for the following thread
            *> to finish
        end-perform
        *> Code executed by the working thread
        ....  a lot of time-consuming work is done here ...
        move 1                 to done

While this might seem reasonable at first glance, the waiting loop is
actually consuming CPU resource without doing any meaningful work
(ie. the CPU is busy while just waiting). It would be much better if
a CBL_THREAD_YIELD was coded in the loop to yield the CPU to the
working thread so it will finish faster. It would be even better to
use one of the synchronization objects, such as an event or a
condition, to signal completion of the work to the waiting thread.
Since, on some operating systems, these objects can be implemented so
that the waiting thread is never awake until the moment when the work
is finished and the event/condition is signaled, this would maximize
CPU utilization.

Deadlock
--------

A deadlock condition is one in which two (or more) threads are
competing for resources that the other has locked. Avoiding or
correcting this situation is not quite as straight forward as dealing
with busy waiting. The following code fragments, executed in two
separate threads, illustrate a potential deadlock situation:

        *> Thread 1 execution         *> Thread 2 execution
        set mutex-1   to on
                                      set mutex-2   to on
                                      set mutex-1   to on
        set mutex-2   to on

Consider the case where thread 1 acquires the lock on mutex-1 and
then gets suspended, at which point thread 2 acquires a lock on
mutex-2. It now does not matter who will execute next, as neither
thread will be able to get their required lock (i.e. thread 1 can't
get a lock on mutex-2, as thread-2 already owns it and similarly for
thread 2 and mutex-1). The application has not provided a way to
release the sequence of locks, so these threads will wait
indefinitely on a condition that will never happen. There are two
methods an application can use to deal with this situation.

To avoid deadlock in the first place, the application can impose on
itself a lock hierarchy. In other words, if the code needs more than
one lock imposed, then the order that those locks are obtained is
always the same. In this case, we could solve the deadlock potential
by making sure that if thread 2 needed locks on mutex-1 and mutex-2
at the same time, then it always acquired mutex-1 before attempting
to acquire mutex-2.

To correct a potential deadlock situation, each thread must be aware
of all locks outstanding at the point another lock is required. When
the next in a sequence of locks is attempted, then the
CBL_MUTEX_ACQUIRE API call must be used and it's flag parameter
should be set to h'01'. This will allow the call to return
immediately with a non-zero return code if the lock cannot be
established. Once the lock attempt has failed, it is up to the
requesting thread to unlock all other locks held to that point and
yield, allowing any other thread to complete its transaction. After
the yielding, the original thread can attempt to reestablish all
locks that were released and attempt to establish the lock that
originally failed.

While the deadlock correction technique might (or might not!) sound
simple, it can be complex to implement properly, as it usually
involves partial 'transaction' roll-backs. Also, in the worst case,
it can lead to two or more threads repeatedly attempting to establish
a lock hierarchy but failing and having to bail out each time. This
can be almost as bad as a deadlock situation (but it is extremely
unlikely that this would happen indefinitely). In general, we would
suggest that you try your best to use avoidance, rather than attempt
to implement correction properly.

Single Thread Deadlock and Writer Starvation
--------------------------------------------

When using monitors to syncronize data access it is possible, via the
CBL_MONITOR_OPEN API call, to specify what method is used to schedule
the granting of read and write access of the monitor. By default,
readers and writers will have interleaved priority. This means that
when a write request is submitted, all following read requests will
block until that write request is granted and subsequently released.
If a further write or browse request is submitted while those
following readers are waiting, then the waiting readers are allowed
access first before that writer is granted access. This is a very
fair scheduling for readers, writers and browsers but, when monitor
read locks are nested in a single thread, there is a possibility of a
single thread deadlock condition arising.

Consider the following code:

        *> Thread 1 execution         *> Thread 2 execution
        set monitor-1 to reading
                                      set monitor-1 to writing
        ... other code + calls ...
        set monitor-1 to reading

When thread 1 acquires a read lock on monitor-1, all further writers
will be blocked until thread 1 releases its lock. Specifically,
thread 2 will be unable to acquire his write lock and, from our
scheduling of readers and writers, thread 2's request will stop any
further read locks from being granted. Now, if thread 1 requests
another (nested) read lock then it will block until thread 2's write
request is granted but, of course, the write request cannot be
granted because thread 1 still has a read lock outstanding. This is
single thread deadlock.

There are a few ways of avoiding this condition. First, if possible,
the programmer can avoid nesting of monitor read locks, but this is
sometimes difficult to arrange in some modular applications.
Alternatively, if a nested lock request is expected, a write or
browse lock can be obtained at the highest level. This approach will
tend to reduce the level of multi-threading through the application,
but it is definitely preferable to a deadlock condition being
encountered. As final option, the programmer can change the
scheduling priority of the monitor in question to readers priority by
specifying the appropriate flag on the CBL_MONITOR_OPEN call.

A readers priority monitor gives absolute priority to read requests
on a monitor. All write requests must wait until no readers are
active on the monitor before being granted access. New readers are
not blocked from access and will continue to prevent outstanding
write requests from being fulfilled. If new read requests are
continually being received on a monitor while at least one has
already been granted, then the writer will never get access to the
resource being requested. This condition is called writer starvation
and can reasonably occur in high read traffic multi-threaded modules.
The only way to avoid writer starvation is to use the interleaved
scheduling priority for monitors and make sure you avoid single
thread deadlock.

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 5

Optimizations & Programming Tips
================================

Throughout this document there are various hints on general coding
and optimizations that allow a multi-threaded application to run as
fast and as flexibly as possible. It is worthwhile recapping them in
point form here:

    o  Always code a check to see the kind of RTS the application is
       running under (i.e. single-threaded or multi-threaded).
    o  Use the NOREENTRANT and NOSERIAL program attributes (the
       default) wherever possible. This minimizes stack space and
       call entry overhead but it is up to the caller to ensure this
       program is only active in one thread at a time.
    o  The use of the REENTRANT program attribute will increase stack
       space usage but will not significantly affect the call speed
       of the program.
    o  The use of the SERIAL program attribute will significantly
       affect call speed but will not affect stack space requirements
       of the program.
    o  Use the LOCAL-STORAGE section to hold thread work variables.
       While this will increase stack space requirements, the
       allocation of data is very fast.
    o  Use of the THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section will significantly
       affect call speed.
    o  Replacing the WORKING-STORAGE SECTION with the
       THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE SECTION will easily allow some
       applications to be made entirely thread-safe.
    o  Use Mutexes for simple locking. These are the fastest of the
       synchronization structures.
    o  Use Monitors to provide maximum multi-threading in
       Readers/Writers based problems.
    o  If possible, initialize all synchronization data items while
       in single threaded execution. If this is not possible, then
       use a first time flag which is checked once before a PROG_LOCK
       and checked again after doing obtaining the lock.
    o  Remember the difference between a thread and a thread handle.
       Create a non-detached thread only if necessary.
    o  Use CBL_THREAD_KILL only if absolutely necessary and if you do
       use it, terminate the run-unit as soon as possible afterwards.
       Use termination polling instead.
    o  Use CBL_THREAD_SUSPEND in preference to CBL_THREAD_YIELD.
       Avoid busy waits.
    o  Keep the amount of work done between CBL_THREAD_LIST_START and
       CBL_THREAD_LIST_END calls to a minimum.
    o  In certain circumstances, the application can conserve per
       thread memory by using the CBL_TSTORE_ API in preference to
       the THREAD-LOCAL-STORAGE section.
    o  Try to avoid deadlock rather than attempting correction once a
       situation has been found.
    o  Avoid nesting read locks on monitors and, if you must, then be
       aware of the possibility of single thread deadlock.

-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Chapter 6

Compiler Directives
===================

There are new compiler directives that can affect the performance of
your multi-threaded and single threaded application.

[no]paramcountcheck     controls checking of number of entry point
                        parameters.

If paramcountcheck is specified, generated code will only take the
number of parameters passed by the caller. The address of all other
linkage section items will be set to NULL. paramcountcheck should
should be used if a program could be called with a variable number of
parameters.

If noparamcountcheck is specified, then generated code takes the
number of parameters expected by the entry point, irrespective of how
many are actually passed. In this case the syntax IF ADDRESS OF
<linkage item> = NULL will not work.

[no]fastcalls           controls the behaviour of a called program.

If fastcalls is specified, the program does not set up information to
allow it to determine whether it is a main program, the RTS will
always assume it is not and EXIT PROGRAM will always exit to the
caller Also, programs do not set up information to allow further
called programs to determine whether they are main programs. Thus,
any program called by a program compiled with fastcalls should itself
be compiled with fastcalls.

[no]reentrant           controls reentrancy of a program

If reentrant is specified, many program areas will be allocated
dynamically, so that it is safe to have multiple copies of the
program running.

[no]serial              controls serialization of a program.

If serial is specified, the Run Time System will determine whether
the program is active in a thread and prevent any other thread
entering the active program. Note that the code to determine this
takes time and is called on every program entry. Programs should only
be compiled with serial if they are going to run in a multi-threaded
environment.

[no]fixopt              controls placing of certain control areas.

If fixopt is specified, the frame pointer will point to the static
data area, allowing data addressing without fixups. This directive
may be necessary on large GNT or COFF programs which exceed the COFF
limit of 65536 relocations. If set, fastlink behaviour is not
available and stack tracing under an object code debugger will also
not work.

[no]fastlink            controls handling of parameters.

If fastlink is specified, and the parameters conform to the
specifications given below, parameters are not unloaded from the
stack, but manipulated in place. The requirements are:

1. The parameters must be in the same order on every entry point
2. This order does not have to be the same order as the order in
   which they appear as 01 items in the linkage section
3. The program must be compiled with nofixopt and notypecheck

The following directive settings are made by default:

PARAMCOUNTCHECK NOFASTCALLS NOREENTRANT NOSERIAL FIXOPT (FASTLINK)

Note that the fastlink will not work, because typecheck and fixopt
are set.

For maximum speed, but with potential semantic changes:

NOTYPECHECK FASTCALLS NOREENTRANT NOSERIAL NOFIXOPT FASTLINK
```
